Refuting the Existence of the Non-Existent and the Intermediate
The author presents a proof against the existence of the non-existent and the intermediate, with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments against the existence of the non-existent and the intermediate, and why some scholars mistakenly affirmed them.

Core Idea
What is not real cannot have existence, and there is no middle state between being and non-being.

The author tackles two confused doctrines: that non-existent things have some kind of external reality, and that some attributes exist in an intermediate state. Although these ideas appear obviously false, many intelligent scholars fell into error. In this passage, the author exposes the logical confusion behind each mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two issues
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two doctrines: the existence of the non-existent and the intermediate (al-wasita).
The author begins by stating the invalidity of both.
2
Understand the objection
An objection is raised: if the invalidity is so clear, why did many intelligent people adopt these views? This sets up the need for explanation.
3
Examine the answer for the first doctrine
The author explains that scholars confused the quiddity (essence) of a thing with its existence. For example, non-existent blackness is still blackness in essence, but that does not mean it exists externally.
The causes of quiddity are different from causes of existence.
4
Examine the answer for the second doctrine
Some attributes like existence, causing, and colorness appear to be neither existent nor non-existent because the existent is described by them regardless of intellectual positing. Scholars concluded they are intermediate.
The author will later show this reasoning is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة بطلان ثبوت المعدوم والحال

قال: فإن قلت بطلان ثبوت المعدوم والواسطة في غاية الجلاء، فكيف ذهب على الكثير من العقلاء؟

قلت: كان مبنى الأول : على أن السواد المعدوم مثلا سواد في الخارج لا يتعلق سواديته بأسباب الوجود .

والثاني: على أن من الصفات ما قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة ولا سبيل إلى نفيها لاتصاف الموجود بها وجد فرض العقل أو لم يوجد، كالوجود والإيجاد والعالمية، واللونية فجزموا بأنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة) .

لما كان بطلان القول بثبوت المعدوم في الخارج، وتحقق الواسطة بينه وبين الموجود جلياً بل ضرورياً، وقد ذهب إليهما سيما إلى تحقق الواسطة العلماء المحققين، حاول التنبيه على ما يصلح مظنة للاشتباه في كثير من المقامين .

أما الأول فهو أن العقل جازم بأن السواد سواد في الواقع، وإن لم يوجد أسباب الوجود من الفاعل والقابل فإن أسباب الماهية غير أسباب الوجود على ما سيجيء فعبروا عن هذا المعنى بالثبوت في الخارج، لما رأوا فيه من شائبة التقرر والتحقق مع نفيهم الوجود الذهني، وهو قريب من قول
Proofs of the invalidity of the existence of the non-existent and the state. He said: If you say: The invalidity of the existence of the non-existent and the intermediate is extremely clear, so how did it escape many intelligent people? I say: The basis of the first is that non-existent blackness, for example, is blackness in the external world, its blackness not being connected to causes of existence. And the second: that among the attributes are those for which proof has been established that they are not existent, yet there is no way to deny them because the existent is qualified by them whether a positing of the intellect occurs or not, like existence, causing, worldliness, and colorness; so they determined that they are neither existent nor non-existent. Since the invalidity of the statement that the non-existent exists in the external world and the realization of an intermediate between it and the existent are clear, indeed necessary, yet verifying scholars have gone to both—especially to the realization of the intermediate—he attempted to point out what might be a source of confusion in many of the two positions. As for the first: the intellect is certain that blackness is blackness in reality, even if the causes of existence from the agent and recipient do not exist, for the causes of quiddity are different from the causes of existence, as will come; so they expressed this meaning by 'existence in the external world' because they saw in it a taint of fixity and realization while denying mental existence, and it is close to the statement
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an argument-and-objection passage in which the author presents the proof for the invalidity of the doctrine that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) exists in the external world and that there is an intermediate (al-wasita) between existence and non-existence. The author begins by stating the heading: 'Proofs of the invalidity of the existence of the non-existent and the state.' Then an objection is raised: if the invalidity of these doctrines is so clear, why did many intelligent people adopt them? The answer explains two bases for the confusion. First, regarding the existence of the non-existent: they considered non-existent blackness to be actually blackness in the external world, not conditioned by the causes of existence; they confused the quiddity (essence) with external existence. Second, regarding the intermediate: some attributes (like existence, causing, worldliness, colorness) are proven to be neither existent nor non-existent because an existent is described by them regardless of intellectual positing; thus they concluded that such attributes occupy an intermediate status. The author then notes that since the invalidity of both doctrines is clear (even necessary), yet some verifying scholars have adopted them, he aims to clarify the source of confusion in both positions. He elaborates on the first confusion: the intellect judges that blackness is blackness in reality even if the causes of existence are absent, because the causes of quiddity differ from causes of existence. The proponents expressed this as 'existence in the external world' because they perceived a sense of fixity and verification, while denying mental existence. The passage ends with 'and it is close to the statement,' indicating continuation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existent things have some form of external reality (existence in the external world) or that there is an intermediate state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The passage argues that such beliefs confuse quiddity (essence) with existence. Non-existent things have no reality; attributes like existence are either existent or non-existent, not intermediate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some scholars mistakenly affirm an intermediate between existence and non-existence?
A) They confused quiddity with existence.
B) They thought attributes like existence must be either existent or non-existent.
C) They argued that some attributes are neither existent nor non-existent because an existent is described by them regardless of intellectual positing.
D) They denied the principle of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a thing is not the same as its existence; confusing the two leads to the false belief that non-existent things can have some reality or that there is an intermediate state.

How can you apply the lesson's refutation of the intermediate state to ensure your self-descriptions are grounded in reality?

Essences and Thingness: The First Argument for Modes
First argument for modes from essence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument of the philosophers for intermediate modes, based on the distinction between impossible and possible non-existents and the concepts of thingness and fixedness.

Core Idea
Possible non-existents have a 'thingness' that makes them distinct from impossibles.

The philosophers begin their argument for intermediate modes by examining the nature of essences. They notice a crucial difference between things that are impossible and things that are merely non-existent but possible. This distinction leads them to posit a kind of reality—thingness—that belongs to possibles even when they do not exist. Let's read their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the philosopher's starting point
Notice that the philosophers begin by stating that essences are not created by a maker. They then observe a distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents.
This distinction is the foundation of their argument.
3
Understand 'thingness' and 'fixedness'
The philosophers use the terms 'shay'iyya' and 'thubūt' to describe the reality that possible non-existents have in the external world, even when they do not exist. This is their first intermediate category.
These terms are key to kalam and philosophy.
4
Relate to the lesson objective
Reflect on how this argument establishes a middle ground between existence and non-existence, which is the central thesis of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلاسفة: أن الماهيات ليست بجعل الجاعل وحاصله: أنهم وجدوا تفرقة بين الممتنعات والمعدومات الممكنة فإن لها ماهيات تتصف بالوجود تارة وتتعرى عنه أخرى بحسب حصول أسباب الوجود ولا حصولها، فعبروا عن ذلك بالشيئية والثبوت في الخارج. وأما الثاني: فهو أنهم وجدوا بعض ما يتصف به الموجود كوجود الإنسان وإيجاد الله تعالى إياه وعالمية زيد ولونية السواد قد قام الدليل على أنه ليس بموجود، ولم يكن لهم سبيل إلى الحكم بأنه لا تحقق له أصلاً لما رأوا الموجودات تتصف به سواء وجد اعتبار العقل أو لم يوجد على أنه لو وجد اعتبار العقل وفرضه فهو عندهم ليس بموجود في العقل، فجزموا بأن لهذا النوع من المعاني تحققاً ما في الخارج، وليست بموجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة، وسموه بالحال توضيحه: أنه إذا صدر المعلول عن العلة، فنحن نجد في كل منهما صفة كانت معدومة قبل الصدور، أعني الموجديــة والوجود، فلا تكون حينئذ معدومة، ضرورة التفرقة بين الحالين وقد قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة فتكون واسطة. قال: وأما ابتناء ذلك على أنهم لم يجعلوا تقابل العدم والوجود، وتقابل السلب والإيجاب، بل العدم والملكة. إذ العدم ارتفاع ما من الوجود، فجعلوا المفهومات الاعتبارية: التي لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها لا موجودة ولا معدومة فإنما يصح ذلك إذا لم يجعل الممتنع معدوماً.
The philosophers [say]: that essences are not by the making of a maker, and its summary: that they found a distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents, for they have essences that are qualified by existence sometimes and stripped of it at other times according to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the causes of existence. So they expressed that by thingness (al-shay'iyya) and fixedness (al-thubūt) in the external [world]. As for the second [argument]: it is that they found some of that by which the existent is qualified, such as the existence (wujūd) of human, God's bringing him into existence, Zayd's being knowledgeable, and the blackness of black, for which proof has been established that it is not existent, yet they had no way to judge that it has no realization (taḥaqquq) at all, because they saw that existents are qualified by it whether the consideration of the intellect exists or not—on the contrary, if the consideration of the intellect and its supposition existed, it is for them not existent in the intellect. So they decided that this type of meanings has a certain realization in the external, and they are neither existent nor non-existent but intermediate, and they named it the mode (al-ḥāl). Its explanation: that when an effect issues from a cause, we find in each of them an attribute that was non-existent before the issuing, namely effect-causing (al-mūjidiyya) and existence (al-wujūd); so it is not then non-existent, due to the necessity of the distinction between the two states, and proof has been established that it is not existent, so it becomes intermediate. He said: And the foundation of that is that they did not make the opposition of non-existence and existence, and the opposition of negation and affirmation, but rather [made it] of privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka). For non-existence is the removal of that which [can] be from existence, so they made the conceptual notions (al-mafhūmāt al-i‘tibāriyya) which do not admit the occurrence of existence to them neither existent nor non-existent. That is only correct if they do not make the impossible non-existent.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' arguments for the existence of an intermediate category between existence and non-existence, called 'the mode' (al-ḥāl). It begins by stating the philosophers' position: that essences (māhiyyāt) are not created by a maker; they have a fixed thingness (shay'iyya) that is distinct from both impossible and possible non-existents. The first argument is based on the distinction between impossible things (al-mumtani‘āt) and possible non-existents (al-ma‘dūmāt al-mumkina): the latter have essences that can receive existence, and this capacity itself is a kind of reality in the external world, which they call 'thingness' and 'fixedness'. The second argument focuses on certain attributes (e.g., the existence of a human, God's act of bringing into existence, Zayd's being knowledgeable, the color black) that are clearly not existent in themselves yet are not simply non-existent, because they are found to qualify existents independently of mental consideration. The philosophers concluded that such meanings have a 'realization' (taḥaqquq) in the external world, but are neither existent nor non-existent; they are intermediate. They call this intermediate entity 'the mode' (al-ḥāl). The passage then clarifies with an example: when a cause produces an effect, both cause and effect acquire new attributes (e.g., being an effect-causer and being an effect) that were non-existent before. These attributes are not non-existent after production, because there is a clear distinction between the before and after states. Yet they are also not existent, because proof shows they are not independently existent. Hence they must be intermediate. Finally, the passage explains the philosophical foundation of this view: the philosophers do not accept a simple opposition of existence and non-existence (affirmation vs. negation), but rather an opposition of 'privation' (al-‘adam) and 'possession' (al-malaka). According to them, non-existence is merely the removal of something that can possibly exist. Consequently, conceptual notions that cannot receive existence at all (e.g., relations, mental considerations) are classified as neither existent nor non-existent. This view is only coherent if one does not classify the impossible as non-existent.

Key Terms
الماهيات
الشيئية
الثبوت
الممتنعات
المعدومات الممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that anything non-existent is simply nothing with no reality at all.
Correction: The philosophers argue that possible non-existents have a thingness (shay'iyya) that distinguishes them from impossibles, giving them a kind of external reality.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what do they express by the terms 'thingness' (shay'iyya) and 'fixedness' (thubūt)?
A) The distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents.
B) The reality that possible non-existents have in the external world.
C) The existence of the maker.
D) The non-existence of essences.

Key Takeaway
Possible non-existents have a thingness that distinguishes them from impossibles, forming a middle ground between existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the fact that before you were born you had a 'thingness' in Allah's knowledge, how does that change the way you view your own existence and purpose? Are you living as if your essence is merely a product of circumstance, or as something that has always been real to Allah?
?
If possible non-existents have a fixedness (thubut) even before they exist, what does that imply about the reality of your future deeds in Allah's decree? How should this affect your intention and resolve to act righteously?
?
The philosophers argue that essences are not created by a maker—they are inherently real. But as a believer, you affirm that everything is created by Allah. How do you reconcile the philosophers' claim with the Islamic teaching that Allah is the Creator of everything, including essences? Does this passage challenge you to deepen your understanding of divine creation?

Attributes and the Intermediate Mode
Second argument for modes from attributes and cause-effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument of the philosophers for modes, which focuses on attributes that qualify existents but are not themselves existent, leading to the concept of the mode (ḥāl) as an intermediate entity.

Core Idea
Attributes like 'being knowledgeable' have a reality that is neither existence nor non-existence.

The philosophers present a second argument based on certain attributes that we find in existent things. For example, Zayd's being knowledgeable or the blackness of black are not themselves existent entities, yet they are not simply non-existent because they have a real effect. This forces the philosophers to posit a third category: the mode (al-ḥāl). Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما الثاني' to the end. Pay attention to the examples given: existence of human, God's bringing into existence, Zayd's being knowledgeable, blackness of black.
2
Analyze the attribute examples
The philosophers argue that these attributes are not existent (since proof shows they are not independently existing) but also not non-existent (since they qualify existents). Why can't they be simply non-existent?
They have a 'realization' (taḥaqquq) in the external world.
3
Understand the cause-effect illustration
The passage uses the example of a cause producing an effect. Before production, the attributes 'effect-causing' and 'existence' were non-existent; after production, they are not non-existent, yet they are not existent either. This illustrates the intermediate status.
The distinction between the two states (before and after) proves they are not non-existent.
4
Grasp the foundation in privation
The philosophers base this on the opposition of privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka), not simple negation. This allows them to classify conceptual notions that cannot receive existence as neither existent nor non-existent.

الفلاسفة: أن الماهيات ليست بجعل الجاعل وحاصله: أنهم وجدوا تفرقة بين الممتنعات والمعدومات الممكنة فإن لها ماهيات تتصف بالوجود تارة وتتعرى عنه أخرى بحسب حصول أسباب الوجود ولا حصولها، فعبروا عن ذلك بالشيئية والثبوت في الخارج. وأما الثاني: فهو أنهم وجدوا بعض ما يتصف به الموجود كوجود الإنسان وإيجاد الله تعالى إياه وعالمية زيد ولونية السواد قد قام الدليل على أنه ليس بموجود، ولم يكن لهم سبيل إلى الحكم بأنه لا تحقق له أصلاً لما رأوا الموجودات تتصف به سواء وجد اعتبار العقل أو لم يوجد على أنه لو وجد اعتبار العقل وفرضه فهو عندهم ليس بموجود في العقل، فجزموا بأن لهذا النوع من المعاني تحققاً ما في الخارج، وليست بموجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة، وسموه بالحال توضيحه: أنه إذا صدر المعلول عن العلة، فنحن نجد في كل منهما صفة كانت معدومة قبل الصدور، أعني الموجديــة والوجود، فلا تكون حينئذ معدومة، ضرورة التفرقة بين الحالين وقد قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة فتكون واسطة. قال: وأما ابتناء ذلك على أنهم لم يجعلوا تقابل العدم والوجود، وتقابل السلب والإيجاب، بل العدم والملكة. إذ العدم ارتفاع ما من الوجود، فجعلوا المفهومات الاعتبارية: التي لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها لا موجودة ولا معدومة فإنما يصح ذلك إذا لم يجعل الممتنع معدوماً.
The philosophers [say]: that essences are not by the making of a maker, and its summary: that they found a distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents, for they have essences that are qualified by existence sometimes and stripped of it at other times according to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the causes of existence. So they expressed that by thingness (al-shay'iyya) and fixedness (al-thubūt) in the external [world]. As for the second [argument]: it is that they found some of that by which the existent is qualified, such as the existence (wujūd) of human, God's bringing him into existence, Zayd's being knowledgeable, and the blackness of black, for which proof has been established that it is not existent, yet they had no way to judge that it has no realization (taḥaqquq) at all, because they saw that existents are qualified by it whether the consideration of the intellect exists or not—on the contrary, if the consideration of the intellect and its supposition existed, it is for them not existent in the intellect. So they decided that this type of meanings has a certain realization in the external, and they are neither existent nor non-existent but intermediate, and they named it the mode (al-ḥāl). Its explanation: that when an effect issues from a cause, we find in each of them an attribute that was non-existent before the issuing, namely effect-causing (al-mūjidiyya) and existence (al-wujūd); so it is not then non-existent, due to the necessity of the distinction between the two states, and proof has been established that it is not existent, so it becomes intermediate. He said: And the foundation of that is that they did not make the opposition of non-existence and existence, and the opposition of negation and affirmation, but rather [made it] of privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka). For non-existence is the removal of that which [can] be from existence, so they made the conceptual notions (al-mafhūmāt al-i‘tibāriyya) which do not admit the occurrence of existence to them neither existent nor non-existent. That is only correct if they do not make the impossible non-existent.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' arguments for the existence of an intermediate category between existence and non-existence, called 'the mode' (al-ḥāl). It begins by stating the philosophers' position: that essences (māhiyyāt) are not created by a maker; they have a fixed thingness (shay'iyya) that is distinct from both impossible and possible non-existents. The first argument is based on the distinction between impossible things (al-mumtani‘āt) and possible non-existents (al-ma‘dūmāt al-mumkina): the latter have essences that can receive existence, and this capacity itself is a kind of reality in the external world, which they call 'thingness' and 'fixedness'. The second argument focuses on certain attributes (e.g., the existence of a human, God's act of bringing into existence, Zayd's being knowledgeable, the color black) that are clearly not existent in themselves yet are not simply non-existent, because they are found to qualify existents independently of mental consideration. The philosophers concluded that such meanings have a 'realization' (taḥaqquq) in the external world, but are neither existent nor non-existent; they are intermediate. They call this intermediate entity 'the mode' (al-ḥāl). The passage then clarifies with an example: when a cause produces an effect, both cause and effect acquire new attributes (e.g., being an effect-causer and being an effect) that were non-existent before. These attributes are not non-existent after production, because there is a clear distinction between the before and after states. Yet they are also not existent, because proof shows they are not independently existent. Hence they must be intermediate. Finally, the passage explains the philosophical foundation of this view: the philosophers do not accept a simple opposition of existence and non-existence (affirmation vs. negation), but rather an opposition of 'privation' (al-‘adam) and 'possession' (al-malaka). According to them, non-existence is merely the removal of something that can possibly exist. Consequently, conceptual notions that cannot receive existence at all (e.g., relations, mental considerations) are classified as neither existent nor non-existent. This view is only coherent if one does not classify the impossible as non-existent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if something is not existent, it must be non-existent.
Correction: The philosophers argue that there are entities (modes) that are neither existent nor non-existent, based on the opposition of privation and possession.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the foundation that allows the philosophers to classify certain attributes as neither existent nor non-existent?
A) The opposition of existence and non-existence.
B) The opposition of negation and affirmation.
C) The opposition of privation and possession.
D) The consideration of the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Attributes like being knowledgeable have a reality that is neither existence nor non-existence, forming the intermediate category of mode (ḥāl).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that certain qualities like 'being knowledgeable' have a reality that is neither existence nor non-existence. Now, consider your own faith: Is your īmān something that simply exists or does not exist, or does it have a deeper, intermediate reality that you cannot reduce to a binary? Where do you place it on this spectrum, and what does that mean for how you nurture it? Commit to examining one quality of your soul this week that seems to have this mode-like reality and see how that changes your approach to it.
?
The philosophers argue that before an effect issues, its attributes are non-existent, and after, they are present but not existent. Think of a relationship you have—say, between you and a loved one. The bond itself is not a physical object, yet it has a real impact and changes over time. Is your relationship merely 'existent' or 'non-existent'? Or does it have an intermediate reality that demands careful maintenance? Resolve to treat one such intangible bond today as having a real but non-physical existence, giving it the attention it deserves.

Philosophers' Basis for Modes and First Objection
Introduction and first objection to the philosophers' modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rationale for positing an intermediate category (modes) and the author's first objection regarding the conflation of non-existent and impossible.

Core Idea
The philosophers' distinction fails if non-existent and impossible are not distinct.

The author begins by summarizing the philosophical position that some concepts can receive existence, while others (like second intelligibles) cannot, leading to a threefold classification. He then declares this view unsatisfactory and raises a first objection concerning the definition of non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the classification
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the philosophers' three categories: existent, non-existent, and neither (modes).
The passage uses 'وجود' and 'عدم' for existence and non-existence.
2
Note the author's dismissal
The author states 'والحق أن هذا الظن لا يغني من الحق شيئاً' — this view avails nothing. Reflect on why he dismisses it so strongly.
3
Analyze the first objection
The first objection requires that 'non-existent' (al-ma‘dūm) be distinct from 'impossible' (al-mumtani‘). Ask yourself: why is this condition necessary?
The author cites al-Talkhīṣ to support that the philosophers' position only holds if these are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول; ذكر صاحب المواقف على ما نطق به أصل النسخ: أنه يظن ظناً قريباً من اليقين، أن مبنى إثبات الواسطة على أنهم وجدوا من المفهومات ما يتصور عروض الوجود لها، فسموا تحققها وجوداً، وارتفاعها عدماً، ومنها ما لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها أصلاً، كالاعتبارات العقلية التي تسميها الحكماء معقولات ثانية، فجعلوها لا موجودة ولا معدومة بمعنى أنها ليست متحققة، ولا من شأنها التحقق فعندنا تقابل الوجود والعدم تقابل إيجاب وسلب. وعندهم تقابل ملكة وعدم. والحق أن هذا الظن لا يغني من الحق شيئاً. أما أولاً: فلأنه إنما يصح لو كان المعدوم عندهم مبايناً للممتنع لا يطلق عليه أصلاً، كما ذكره صاحب التلخيص: لا أعم على ما قرره صاحب المواقف وغيره لظهور أنه لا يعرض له الوجود أصلاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن الحال حينئذ تكون أبعد عن الوجود من المعدوم، لما أنه ليس له التحقق ولا إمكان التحقق وليس كذلك لما أنهم يجعلونه قد تجاوز في التقرر والتحقق والثبوت حد العدم، ولم يبلغ حد الوجود، ولهذا جوزوا كونه جزءاً لموجود كلونية السواد. وأما ثالثاً: فلأنه ينافي ما ذكره في تفسير الواسطة، في أنه المعلوم الذي له تحقق لا باعتبار ذاته، بل تبعاً لغيره، أو الكائن في الأعيان، لا بالاستقلال بل تبعاً لغيره، ويمكن دفع الأخيرين بأن المراد بالتحقق الذي يتصور عروضه للمعدوم دون الواسطة هو التحقق بالاستقلال، وأن الواسطة تكون أقرب إلى الوجود من حيث أن التحقق بالتبعة حاصل له بالفعل.
I say: The author of al-Mawāqif mentioned, according to what the original copy states: that he thinks, with a thought near certainty, that the basis for affirming the intermediate is that they found among concepts some that admit the occurrence of existence to them, so they called their realization (taḥaqquq) existence, and their removal non-existence; and among them are those that do not admit the occurrence of existence at all, like the intellectual considerations which the sages call second intelligibles. So they made them neither existent nor non-existent, meaning that they are not realized, nor is it their nature to be realized. For us, the opposition of existence and non-existence is an opposition of affirmation and negation. For them, it is an opposition of possession (malaka) and privation (adam). The truth is that this opinion avails nothing of the truth. As for the first [objection]: it is only valid if the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm) for them is distinct from the impossible (al-mumtani‘) and is not applied to it at all, as the author of al-Talkhīs mentioned: not more general, according to what the author of al-Mawāqif and others established, because it is evident that existence does not occur to it at all. As for the second [objection]: then the mode (al-ḥāl) would be further from existence than the non-existent, because it has neither realization nor possibility of realization, and it is not like that because they make it to have surpassed in fixity, realization, and stability the limit of non-existence, and not reached the limit of existence. For this reason they permitted it to be a part of an existent, like the blackness of black. As for the third [objection]: it contradicts what he mentioned in the interpretation of the intermediate, namely that it is the known that has realization not by virtue of itself but as dependent on another, or that which is in external things, not independently but dependently. And it is possible to rebut the latter two [objections] by saying that what is meant by 'realization' that can occur to the non-existent but not to the intermediate is realization independently, and that the intermediate is closer to existence in that realization by dependency (al-taḥaqquq bi-l-taba‘iyya) is actually obtained for it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of the philosophers' concept of the intermediate mode (al-ḥāl). The author begins by quoting the view attributed to the author of al-Mawāqif: that the basis for affirming an intermediate category is the distinction between concepts that can receive existence (which they call existent or non-existent) and those that cannot (e.g., second intelligibles), which are then deemed neither existent nor non-existent. The philosophers accordingly replace the opposition of existence and non-existence (affirmation and negation) with the opposition of possession (malaka) and privation (adam). The author declares that this opinion is of no real value. He then raises three objections: (1) The philosophers' distinction between 'non-existent' and 'impossible' is not valid unless 'non-existent' is completely distinct from 'impossible'. But in fact, 'impossible' cannot receive existence at all, so this undermines their categorization. (2) If the mode is further removed from existence than the non-existent (since it has no possibility of realization), this contradicts their own claim that the mode has a kind of fixity and stability beyond non-existence. For they treat modes as parts of existents (e.g., the blackness of black), which implies they are closer to existence. (3) Their definition of the intermediate as something that has realization dependently (bi-l-taba‘iyya) contradicts their own description of modes as having surpassed non-existence without reaching existence. Finally, the author notes a possible rebuttal to the last two objections: that the term 'realization' meant for the non-existent is independent realization, while the mode has realization by dependency, which actually makes it closer to existence. However, the author does not endorse this rebuttal; he merely reports it.

الحال
المعدوم
الممتنع
الملكة
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers' tripartite division is coherent and unproblematic.
Correction: The author shows that the division rests on a flawed assumption about the distinctness of non-existent and impossible.

Checkpoint
What condition does the author say must hold for the philosophers' position to be valid?
A) That the mode be further from existence than the non-existent.
B) That the non-existent be distinct from the impossible.
C) That the mode be part of an existent.
D) That realization be independent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' classification of modes depends on a flawed separation between non-existent and impossible; without it, their system lacks coherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a distinction between two categories (like 'non-existent' and 'impossible') that seemed clear until you realized they overlapped? How does this lesson's exposure of a flawed dichotomy challenge you to examine your own assumptions about what is 'true' or 'real'?
?
If the philosophers' system crumbles because their basic classification is incoherent, what systems or beliefs in your own life might be built on distinctions that don't actually hold? Are you willing to test them as rigorously as the author tests the philosophers' modes?
?
The author says the philosophers' opinion 'avails nothing of the truth.' When have you held an opinion that comforted you but had no real foundation? What would it take for you to abandon it in pursuit of the truth, even if it leaves you without a neat system?

Second Objection: Modes Further from Existence than Non-Existence
Second objection to the philosophers' modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's second objection, which shows the inconsistency of the philosophers' claim that modes are closer to existence than non-existence.

Core Idea
The philosophers' mode cannot be both beyond non-existence and devoid of realization.

The author's second objection targets the ontological status of the mode. He argues that if the mode has no possibility of realization, it is further from existence than the non-existent, contradicting the philosophers' own description.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection carefully
Read the Arabic text for the second objection. Note the logical structure: 'إذا كان الحال لا تحقق له ولا إمكان تحقق فإنه أبعد عن الوجود من المعدوم'.
The Arabic uses 'لما أنه ليس له التحقق ولا إمكان التحقق'.
2
Identify the inconsistency
The philosophers claim the mode has surpassed the limit of non-existence, yet it cannot be realized. This is contradictory. Reflect on why.
3
Consider the example
The author gives the example of blackness (al-sawād) as a part of black. How does this illustrate the philosophers' claim?
The mode is like an ingredient in an existent, but with no independent existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول; ذكر صاحب المواقف على ما نطق به أصل النسخ: أنه يظن ظناً قريباً من اليقين، أن مبنى إثبات الواسطة على أنهم وجدوا من المفهومات ما يتصور عروض الوجود لها، فسموا تحققها وجوداً، وارتفاعها عدماً، ومنها ما لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها أصلاً، كالاعتبارات العقلية التي تسميها الحكماء معقولات ثانية، فجعلوها لا موجودة ولا معدومة بمعنى أنها ليست متحققة، ولا من شأنها التحقق فعندنا تقابل الوجود والعدم تقابل إيجاب وسلب. وعندهم تقابل ملكة وعدم. والحق أن هذا الظن لا يغني من الحق شيئاً. أما أولاً: فلأنه إنما يصح لو كان المعدوم عندهم مبايناً للممتنع لا يطلق عليه أصلاً، كما ذكره صاحب التلخيص: لا أعم على ما قرره صاحب المواقف وغيره لظهور أنه لا يعرض له الوجود أصلاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن الحال حينئذ تكون أبعد عن الوجود من المعدوم، لما أنه ليس له التحقق ولا إمكان التحقق وليس كذلك لما أنهم يجعلونه قد تجاوز في التقرر والتحقق والثبوت حد العدم، ولم يبلغ حد الوجود، ولهذا جوزوا كونه جزءاً لموجود كلونية السواد. وأما ثالثاً: فلأنه ينافي ما ذكره في تفسير الواسطة، في أنه المعلوم الذي له تحقق لا باعتبار ذاته، بل تبعاً لغيره، أو الكائن في الأعيان، لا بالاستقلال بل تبعاً لغيره، ويمكن دفع الأخيرين بأن المراد بالتحقق الذي يتصور عروضه للمعدوم دون الواسطة هو التحقق بالاستقلال، وأن الواسطة تكون أقرب إلى الوجود من حيث أن التحقق بالتبعة حاصل له بالفعل.
I say: The author of al-Mawāqif mentioned, according to what the original copy states: that he thinks, with a thought near certainty, that the basis for affirming the intermediate is that they found among concepts some that admit the occurrence of existence to them, so they called their realization (taḥaqquq) existence, and their removal non-existence; and among them are those that do not admit the occurrence of existence at all, like the intellectual considerations which the sages call second intelligibles. So they made them neither existent nor non-existent, meaning that they are not realized, nor is it their nature to be realized. For us, the opposition of existence and non-existence is an opposition of affirmation and negation. For them, it is an opposition of possession (malaka) and privation (adam). The truth is that this opinion avails nothing of the truth. As for the first [objection]: it is only valid if the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm) for them is distinct from the impossible (al-mumtani‘) and is not applied to it at all, as the author of al-Talkhīs mentioned: not more general, according to what the author of al-Mawāqif and others established, because it is evident that existence does not occur to it at all. As for the second [objection]: then the mode (al-ḥāl) would be further from existence than the non-existent, because it has neither realization nor possibility of realization, and it is not like that because they make it to have surpassed in fixity, realization, and stability the limit of non-existence, and not reached the limit of existence. For this reason they permitted it to be a part of an existent, like the blackness of black. As for the third [objection]: it contradicts what he mentioned in the interpretation of the intermediate, namely that it is the known that has realization not by virtue of itself but as dependent on another, or that which is in external things, not independently but dependently. And it is possible to rebut the latter two [objections] by saying that what is meant by 'realization' that can occur to the non-existent but not to the intermediate is realization independently, and that the intermediate is closer to existence in that realization by dependency (al-taḥaqquq bi-l-taba‘iyya) is actually obtained for it.

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of the philosophers' concept of the intermediate mode (al-ḥāl). The author begins by quoting the view attributed to the author of al-Mawāqif: that the basis for affirming an intermediate category is the distinction between concepts that can receive existence (which they call existent or non-existent) and those that cannot (e.g., second intelligibles), which are then deemed neither existent nor non-existent. However, the author does not endorse this rebuttal; he merely reports it.

Third Objection and a Possible Rebuttal
Third objection and possible rebuttal to the philosophers' modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection, which shows that the philosophers' definition of the intermediate conflicts with their account of modes, and you will also consider a possible rebuttal.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own definition of the intermediate undermines their characterization of modes.

The author's third objection points to a direct contradiction between the philosophers' definition of the intermediate and their statement about modes. He then mentions a potential defense for the latter two objections, though he does not endorse it.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic text for the third objection. Notice how it contrasts with the previous objections.
2
Compare definition and description
The philosophers define the intermediate as something with dependent realization. Their description of modes as beyond non-existence but not reaching existence contradicts this. How?
3
Examine the possible rebuttal
The rebuttal suggests that 'realization' when applied to the non-existent means independent realization, while the intermediate has dependent realization. Does this resolve the contradiction?
The author does not endorse the rebuttal; he merely reports it.

أقول; ذكر صاحب المواقف على ما نطق به أصل النسخ: أنه يظن ظناً قريباً من اليقين، أن مبنى إثبات الواسطة على أنهم وجدوا من المفهومات ما يتصور عروض الوجود لها، فسموا تحققها وجوداً، وارتفاعها عدماً، ومنها ما لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها أصلاً، كالاعتبارات العقلية التي تسميها الحكماء معقولات ثانية، فجعلوها لا موجودة ولا معدومة بمعنى أنها ليست متحققة، ولا من شأنها التحقق فعندنا تقابل الوجود والعدم تقابل إيجاب وسلب. وعندهم تقابل ملكة وعدم. والحق أن هذا الظن لا يغني من الحق شيئاً. أما أولاً: فلأنه إنما يصح لو كان المعدوم عندهم مبايناً للممتنع لا يطلق عليه أصلاً، كما ذكره صاحب التلخيص: لا أعم على ما قرره صاحب المواقف وغيره لظهور أنه لا يعرض له الوجود أصلاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن الحال حينئذ تكون أبعد عن الوجود من المعدوم، لما أنه ليس له التحقق ولا إمكان التحقق وليس كذلك لما أنهم يجعلونه قد تجاوز في التقرر والتحقق والثبوت حد العدم، ولم يبلغ حد الوجود، ولهذا جوزوا كونه جزءاً لموجود كلونية السواد. وأما ثالثاً: فلأنه ينافي ما ذكره في تفسير الواسطة، في أنه المعلوم الذي له تحقق لا باعتبار ذاته، بل تبعاً لغيره، أو الكائن في الأعيان، لا بالاستقلال بل تبعاً لغيره، ويمكن دفع الأخيرين بأن المراد بالتحقق الذي يتصور عروضه للمعدوم دون الواسطة هو التحقق بالاستقلال، وأن الواسطة تكون أقرب إلى الوجود من حيث أن التحقق بالتبعة حاصل له بالفعل.
I say: The author of al-Mawāqif mentioned, according to what the original copy states: that he thinks, with a thought near certainty, that the basis for affirming the intermediate is that they found among concepts some that admit the occurrence of existence to them, so they called their realization (taḥaqquq) existence, and their removal non-existence; and among them are those that do not admit the occurrence of existence at all, like the intellectual considerations which the sages call second intelligibles. So they made them neither existent nor non-existent, meaning that they are not realized, nor is it their nature to be realized. For us, the opposition of existence and non-existence is an opposition of affirmation and negation. For them, it is an opposition of possession (malaka) and privation (adam). The truth is that this opinion avails nothing of the truth. As for the first [objection]: it is only valid if the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm) for them is distinct from the impossible (al-mumtani‘) and is not applied to it at all, as the author of al-Talkhīs mentioned: not more general, according to what the author of al-Mawāqif and others established, because it is evident that existence does not occur to it at all. As for the second [objection]: then the mode (al-ḥāl) would be further from existence than the non-existent, because it has neither realization nor possibility of realization, and it is not like that because they make it to have surpassed in fixity, realization, and stability the limit of non-existence, and not reached the limit of existence. For this reason they permitted it to be a part of an existent, like the blackness of black. As for the third [objection]: it contradicts what he mentioned in the interpretation of the intermediate, namely that it is the known that has realization not by virtue of itself but as dependent on another, or that which is in external things, not independently but dependently. And it is possible to rebut the latter two [objections] by saying that what is meant by 'realization' that can occur to the non-existent but not to the intermediate is realization independently, and that the intermediate is closer to existence in that realization by dependency (al-taḥaqquq bi-l-taba‘iyya) is actually obtained for it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of the philosophers' concept of the intermediate mode (al-ḥāl). The author begins by quoting the view attributed to the author of al-Mawāqif: that the basis for affirming an intermediate category is the distinction between concepts that can receive existence (which they call existent or non-existent) and those that cannot (e.g., second intelligibles), which are then deemed neither existent nor non-existent. The philosophers accordingly replace the opposition of existence and non-existence (affirmation and negation) with the opposition of possession (malaka) and privation (adam). The author declares that this opinion is of no real value. He then raises three objections: (1) The philosophers' distinction between 'non-existent' and 'impossible' is not valid unless 'non-existent' is completely distinct from 'impossible'. But in fact, 'impossible' cannot receive existence at all, so this undermines their categorization. (2) If the mode is further removed from existence than the non-existent (since it has no possibility of realization), this contradicts their own claim that the mode has a kind of fixity and stability beyond non-existence. For they treat modes as parts of existents (e.g., the blackness of black), which implies they are closer to existence. (3) Their definition of the intermediate as something that has realization dependently (bi-l-taba‘iyya) contradicts their own description of modes as having surpassed non-existence without reaching existence. Finally, the author notes a possible rebuttal to the last two objections: that the term 'realization' meant for the non-existent is independent realization, while the mode has realization by dependency, which actually makes it closer to existence. However, the author does not endorse this rebuttal; he merely reports it.

التحقق
الاستقلال
التبعية
الأعيان

Checkpoint

A) Independent
B) Dependent
C) Impossible
D) None

Key Takeaway
The third objection highlights a tension in the philosophers' definition of the intermediate, though a rebuttal is suggested that distinguishes independent from dependent realization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, upon examination, contradicted its own definition? How did you resolve the tension? This lesson shows a philosophical contradiction. Reflect on a time your own understanding of a concept (like faith or intention) had an internal inconsistency. What did you do: did you adjust the definition or the description? Be honest.
?
The philosophers' possible rebuttal distinguishes independent from dependent realization. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes treat your good deeds as independently valuable (like independent realization) when they are truly dependent on Allah's acceptance? How does that distinction change how you view your actions?
?
The intermediate is described as being closer to existence even though it lacks independence. Where in your relationship with Allah do you find yourself in an 'intermediate' state — not fully disconnected but not fully connected? What would it mean to move from dependent realization to a more independent reliance on Allah?

The Criterion for True Judgment
The author establishes that truth depends on correspondence to the thing in itself, rejecting external and mental criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the truth of a judgment is determined by its correspondence to the thing in itself (nafs al-amr), not by external existence or mental representation.

Core Idea
Truth is measured against the thing's own essence, not its external existence or our mental image.

This passage is crucial because it defines the standard for evaluating truth in kalam. The author rejects two common criteria and establishes the objective standard of 'what is in itself'. Let's see how he argues this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the author's concise argument style.
Pay attention to the reasons given for rejecting the two criteria.
2
Identify the two rejected criteria and the accepted one
List the three possibilities: correspondence to external objects, correspondence to mental representations, and correspondence to nafs al-amr. Note why the first two are insufficient.
The author gives specific reasons for each rejection.
3
Understand the meaning of 'nafs al-amr'
The author defines it as 'what is understood from saying 'this thing is such in itself''—the essence of the thing abstracted from any judgment. Reflect on how this concept avoids the problems of the other two criteria.
This is the central technical term of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا يشترط في صحة المطابقة لما في الأعيان، إذ قد لا يوجد فيها الطرفان، ولا يكفي المطابقة لما في الأذهان، اذ قد يرتسم فيها الكواذب، بل المعتبر المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر، ومعناه ما يفهم من قولنا هذا الأمر كذا في نفسه أي في حد ذاته مع قطع النظر عن حكم
He said: It is not a condition for validity that [the judgment] correspond to what is in external objects, for sometimes neither of the two terms [subject and predicate] may exist in them. Nor is correspondence to what is in minds sufficient, for falsehoods may be depicted in them. Rather, what is considered is correspondence to what is in itself (fi nafs al-amr). Its meaning is what is understood from our saying 'this thing is such in itself', i.e., in its own essence, regardless of any judgment.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage sets the criterion for the truth or validity of a judgment (صحة الحكم). The author is discussing the condition for a proposition to be true. He denies two possible criteria: a) Correspondence to external reality (ما في الأعيان): this is not always possible because sometimes the subject or predicate do not exist externally (e.g., 'The phoenix is a mythical bird'—the phoenix does not exist externally). b) Correspondence to mental representations (ما في الأذهان): this is also insufficient because false beliefs can be in the mind (e.g., the philosophers' belief in the eternity of the world). Therefore, the correct standard is correspondence to 'ما في نفس الأمر' (what is in itself), i.e., the objective reality of the thing as it is in its own essence, independent of any judgment about it. The phrase 'في نفس الأمر' is a technical term in Islamic philosophy and kalam referring to the actual state of affairs, the thing as it is in itself, whether in external existence or in mental existence, but considered absolutely. The author clarifies that it means 'this thing is such in itself', i.e., in its own essence, abstracting from any external or mental consideration.

Key Terms
صحة
مطابقة
أعيان
أذهان
نفس الأمر
كواذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that truth is merely correspondence to external objects, but this overlooks judgments about non-existents.
Correction: The passage shows that truth is about the thing's own essence (nafs al-amr), not its external instantiation, because judgments can be true even if the terms lack external existence.

Checkpoint
Why is correspondence to external objects not a necessary condition for a true judgment, according to this passage?
A) Because external objects always exist.
B) Because the terms of the judgment may not exist in external reality.
C) Because external objects are not knowable.
D) Because judgment is only about mental entities.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a judgment is determined by its correspondence to the actual state of the thing itself (nafs al-amr), not its external existence or mental representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself that might be false. Are you measuring it against what is true in itself (nafs al-amr), or against what you prefer to believe? Will you examine that belief today?
?
When you disagree with someone, do you assume your judgment is true because it's in your mind? The passage says truth is not correspondence to mental representations. When was the last time you questioned whether your mental picture matches the thing itself, not just your opinion?
?
The criterion for truth is the thing as it is in its own essence, regardless of any judgment. Is there an area of your life where you are avoiding the truth because it would challenge your self-image? What would it mean to submit to the truth about yourself?

The Impossibility of Abstract Quiddity's Existence
Refutation of objections to abstract quiddity's impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abstract quiddity cannot exist in external entities or in the mind, and how the author refutes two objections that attempt to affirm its mental existence.

Core Idea
Abstract quiddity is impossible because existence and being-in-the-mind are accidents inseparable from it.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle metaphysical problem: whether quiddity (the essence of a thing) can exist stripped of all accidents. He argues that such abstract quiddity cannot exist in the external world because existence and individuation are themselves accidents. He then refutes two objections that claim abstract quiddity can exist in the mind, showing that even mental existence is an accident that cannot be removed.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the main claim
Read the passage to see the author's core argument: abstract quiddity cannot exist externally because existence and individuation are accidents, and cannot exist mentally because being-in-the-mind is also an accident.
Notice that the author establishes impossibility in both realms.
2
Examine the first objection
Some argued that if we restrict 'accidents' to external ones, abstract quiddity could exist in the mind. The author refutes this by noting that being-in-the-mind is itself an accident, so the impossibility remains.
The author also points out that this view makes the status of existence itself problematic.
3
Analyze the second objection and its two proofs
Others claimed that the mind can conceive quiddity without any accidents, either by observing it alone or by considering non-existence of everything. The author refutes both: the first does not yield 'with the condition of nothing', and the second produces a non-matching conception, not real abstraction.
The crucial point is that mental conception does not create actual abstraction; it is merely a flawed representation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

something from the accidents and is called abstract, and quiddity with the condition of nothing, and there is no obscurity in the impossibility of its existence in external entities, because existence is among the accidents, and likewise individuation; and also in minds, whether the accidents are unrestricted or restricted to external ones, because being in the mind is also among the accidents that attached to the mental form, according to external [reality], not by mere consideration of the mind and its making it a description and a restriction of it. And some claimed that its existence in the mind is permissible, if the external accidents are restricted, claiming that being in the mind is among the mental accidents, as if he meant by external accidents what attaches to things occurring in external entities, and by mental accidents what attaches to things subsisting in minds. According to this, then the fact that existence is among the external accidents is subject to consideration based on its precedence in the investigation of existence, so the impossibility of existence of the abstract in external is not realized either. And some mentioned that it exists without restricting the accidents to external and explained it by two ways: in the minds. One of them: that the mind can observe the quiddity alone without observing anything with it. It was refuted by saying that such a thing is not taken with the condition of nothing, and that is clear. And the second: that the mind can consider the non-existence of everything, even its own non-existence, so it is permissible that it considers the quiddity abstract from all accidents, even from being in the mind, even though it is in itself conjoined with them. It was refuted by saying that this does not necessitate its being abstract; rather, the utmost is that the mind has conceived it as such, a non-matching conception (footnote 6). (Footnotes on textual variants and definition of correspondence.)
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage refutes objections regarding the existence of abstract quiddity (الماهية المجردة). The author first reaffirms that abstract quiddity cannot exist in external entities because existence and individuation are accidents, and it cannot exist in the mind either because being in the mind is itself an accident that attaches to the mental form in accordance with external reality, not by mere mental consideration. Some argued that if we restrict 'accidents' to external ones, then mental existence is not an external accident, so the abstract quiddity could exist in the mind. The author replies that this view is problematic because existence itself is an external accident according to its earlier investigation, so impossibility persists. Others provided two arguments for the existence of abstract quiddity in the mind without restriction: 1) The mind can observe quiddity alone—replied that such observation does not mean the quiddity is taken with the condition of nothing; 2) The mind can consider the non-existence of everything, including itself, so it can conceive quiddity abstracted from all accidents—replied that this is a non-matching conception, not actual abstraction. The footnotes provide textual variants and define 'correspondence' (المطابقة).

Correction: The author shows that mental existence itself is an accident, so the quiddity cannot be abstracted from it. Moreover, mental abstraction does not create real abstraction; it only results in a non-matching conception.

Key Takeaway
Abstract quiddity cannot exist because both external existence and mental being are accidents that cannot be removed; mental abstraction is merely a non-matching conception.

?

The Logical Impossibility of Abstract Universals
A concise logical refutation of the existence of abstract universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of abstract universals (al-mujarrada) is logically contradictory according to classical Ash'ari reasoning.

Core Idea
Abstract universals would require existence to be an accident of what is accident-free – a contradiction.

This lesson examines the first part of the passage where the author presents a concise logical argument against the existence of abstract universals. The argument hinges on the relationship between essence (mahiyya), accidents, and existence. We will see why claiming an abstract universal is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the logical structure.
2
Identify the premise
The author states that anything receptive of opposites or part of individuals must be characterized by accidents. Understand this premise.
This is based on the nature of material existence.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'mahiyya la bi-shart shay'' and 'bi-shart la shay'' – these are technical terms from logic.
They describe how essence is considered.
4
Grasp the contradiction
If abstract universals exist, they would be free from accidents, yet existence is an accident. This is contradictory.
The author offers two possible escapes but shows they are problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان هذا ظاهر البطلان. بناء على أن القابل للمتقابلات، والجزء من الأشخاص يتصف بالعوارض لا محالة وأنه هو الماهية لا بشرط شيء الماهية بشرط لا شيء ، وأن الوجود من العوارض . فالقول بوجود المجردة تناقض . اللهم إلا أن تقيد العوارض بغير الوجود، أو يجعل الوجود نفس الماهية . قال الفارابي في كتاب الجمع بين رأي أفلاطون وأرسطو أنه إشارة إلى أن للموجودات صوراً في علم الله تعالى باقية لا تتبدل ولا تتغير. وقال صاحب الإشراق وغيره، أنه إشارة إلى ما عليه الحكماء المتألهون، من أن لكل نوع من الأفلاك والكواكب والبسائط العنصرية ومركباتها جوهراً مجرداً من عالم العقول يدبر أمره، حتى إن الذي لنوع النار هو الذي يحفظها وينورها، ويجذب الدهن والشمع إليها، ويسمونه رب النوع، ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع بملك الجبال وملك البحار ونحو ذلك ومع الاعتراف بكونه جزئياً يقولون. إنه كلي ذلك النوع، بمعنى أن نسبة فيضه إلى جميع على السواء، لا بمعنى أنه مشترك بينها حتى يلزم أن تكون إنسانية مجردة، موجودة في الأعيان مشتركة بين جميع الأفراد محققة في المواد، فيكون هناك إنسان محسوس فاسد آخر معقول مجرد دائم لا يتغير أبداً. ثم للمثل المعلقة التي يسمونها عالم الأشباح المجردة، فإنها لا تكون من الجواهر المجردة، بل كالواسطة بين المحسوس والمعقول، ولا تختص بأنواع الأجسام. بل يكون لكل شخص من الجواهر والأعراض على ما صرح بذلك صاحب الاشراق. فقال: والصور المعلقة ليست مثل أفلاطون. لأن مثل أفلاطون نورية . أي من عالم العقل، وهذه مثل مغلقة من عالم الأشباح المجردة منها ظلمانية، ومنها مستنيرة، وذكر أن لكل نوع من الفلكيات والعنصريات التي في عالم المثل أيضاً، رب نوع من عالم العقول، وأن رب النوع إنما يكون للأنواع الجسمانية المستقلة، وتدبير الأعراض والأجزاء مفوض إلى رب النوع الذي هو محلها من الأجسام. مثلاً في عالم العقل . جوهر مجرد له هيئات نورية، إذا ظله وقع في هذا العالم يكون منه المسك رائحته ، أو السكر مع طعمه، أو الإنسان مع اختلاف أعضائه.
And since this is clearly false, based on the fact that that which is receptive of opposites, and the part of individuals, is necessarily characterized by accidents, and that it is the essence (al-mahiyya) without condition (la bi-shart shay') and the essence with the condition of nothing (bi-shart la shay'), and that existence (al-wujud) is among the accidents. So the statement that the abstract exists is a contradiction. Unless accidents are restricted to other than existence, or existence is made identical to the essence. Al-Farabi said in his book The Harmony Between the Opinions of Plato and Aristotle that it is an indication that existents have forms in the knowledge of God Almighty, remaining, not changing nor altering. And the author of al-Ishraq (Suhrawardi) and others said that it is an indication of what the divine philosophers hold: that for every species of the celestial spheres, planets, elemental simples, and their compounds, there is an abstract substance from the world of intellects that manages its affair, so that which belongs to the species of fire is that which preserves it and illuminates it, and draws oil and wax to it, and they call it the lord of the species (rabb al-naw'), and it is expressed in the language of the Law as the angel of mountains, the angel of seas, and the like. And while acknowledging that it is particular, they say it is universal for that species, meaning that the proportion of its emanation to all is equal, not meaning that it is shared among them such that it would necessitate an abstract humanity existing in individuals, common to all individuals, realized in matters, so that there would be another sensuous perishable human and an abstract perpetual intellect never changing. Then the suspended forms (al-muthul al-mu'allaqa) which they call the world of abstract phantoms (al-ashbah al-mujarrada) are not among the abstract substances, but are like intermediaries between the sensible and the intelligible, and are not specific to the species of bodies. Rather, for every individual of substances and accidents, as stated by the author of al-Ishraq. He said: the suspended forms are not like Plato's forms, for Plato's forms are luminous, i.e., from the world of intellect, while these are suspended forms from the world of abstract phantoms, some dark, some illuminated. And he mentioned that for every species of celestial and elemental things in the world of forms too, there is a lord of the species from the world of intellects, and that the lord of the species is only for independent corporeal species, and the management of accidents and parts is delegated to the lord of the species which is their locus from the bodies. For example, in the world of intellect: an abstract substance having luminous configurations; when its shadow falls into this world, from it comes musk with its scent, or sugar with its taste, or man with his different limbs.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and exposition. It begins by stating that the claim of existence of abstract universals (al-mujarrada) is clearly false, because anything that can receive opposites and is a part of individuals is necessarily characterized by accidents. The essence when considered unconditionally (la bi-shart shay') or with the condition of nothing (bi-shart la shay') is still subject to accidents, and existence itself is an accident. Therefore, claiming the existence of an abstract universal is a contradiction, unless accidents are restricted to non-existence or existence is made identical to the essence. The passage then turns to the interpretations of Plato's Ideas by Al-Farabi and Suhrawardi. Al-Farabi says they are images of existents in God's knowledge. Suhrawardi and others say they are abstract substances from the world of intellects that manage each species, calling them 'lord of the species' (rabb al-naw'), often identified with angels in religious language. They claim these are particular yet universal in terms of equal emanation, not in terms of being shared among individuals—otherwise there would be an abstract humanity existing alongside sensuous humans. The passage then discusses the 'suspended forms' (al-muthul al-mu'allaqa), which are not abstract substances but intermediates between sensible and intelligible, and are particular to individuals. Suhrawardi distinguishes them from Plato's luminous forms, describing them as shadowy and luminous from the world of phantoms. The passage ends with an example of how these forms manifest in the world.

Key Terms
المجردة
الماهية
العوارض
الوجود
لا بشرط شيء
بشرط لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think abstract universals exist as separate entities in a Platonic realm.
Correction: The author shows this is logically impossible because existence (an accident) cannot inhere in something that is not receptive to accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that abstract universals exist a contradiction?
A) Because abstract universals would be composed of matter.
B) Because existence is an accident and abstract universals cannot receive accidents.
C) Because the essence with condition of nothing cannot exist.
D) Because Al-Farabi rejected them.

Key Takeaway
The existence of abstract universals is logically impossible because it would require an accident (existence) to inhere in something that is not receptive to accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of abstract concepts like 'humanity' or 'justice', do you automatically assume they have some kind of real existence outside your mind? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your understanding of God's attributes if existence were not an accident but identical to essence?
?
This proof shows that asking for an abstract universal to exist externally leads to a logical contradiction. Have you ever held a belief that seemed self-evident but upon closer examination turned out to be inconsistent? What would it take for you to abandon a cherished belief when logic demands it?
?
The author mentions two escape routes – restricting accidents or making existence identical to essence. Neither works for created things. Are you tempted to take an easy way out when faced with a difficult truth about yourself? What is one area of your life where you are avoiding a logical implication about your own character or actions?

Interpretations of Platonic Forms: Farabi and Suhrawardi
Exposition of Al-Farabi's and Suhrawardi's interpretations of Platonic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Farabi and Suhrawardi interpreted Plato's Ideas, and the concept of 'lord of the species' and 'suspended forms'.

Core Idea
Platonic forms are not abstract universals; they are either in God's knowledge or particular spiritual entities.

The author now moves from the refutation to discussing alternative interpretations of what Plato's Ideas might actually refer to. Al-Farabi suggests they are forms in God's knowledge, while Suhrawardi proposes a hierarchical world of intellects and suspended forms. This lesson explores these views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Farabi passage
Read Al-Farabi's interpretation: the Ideas are forms in God's knowledge, unchanging.
2
Read the Suhrawardi passage
Read Suhrawardi's view: there are abstract substances (lords of species) that manage each species, and suspended forms that are intermediate.
Note the distinction from Plato's luminous forms.
3
Compare the two views
Compare how each author avoids the problem of abstract universals by placing these forms in a non-material context.
Farabi places them in God's mind; Suhrawardi makes them particular substances.
4
Understand 'lord of the species'
This concept is a particular intellect that emanates to all individuals equally, not a universal shared among them.
This avoids the logical problem from lesson 1.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان هذا ظاهر البطلان. بناء على أن القابل للمتقابلات، والجزء من الأشخاص يتصف بالعوارض لا محالة وأنه هو الماهية لا بشرط شيء الماهية بشرط لا شيء ، وأن الوجود من العوارض . فالقول بوجود المجردة تناقض . اللهم إلا أن تقيد العوارض بغير الوجود، أو يجعل الوجود نفس الماهية . قال الفارابي في كتاب الجمع بين رأي أفلاطون وأرسطو أنه إشارة إلى أن للموجودات صوراً في علم الله تعالى باقية لا تتبدل ولا تتغير. وقال صاحب الإشراق وغيره، أنه إشارة إلى ما عليه الحكماء المتألهون، من أن لكل نوع من الأفلاك والكواكب والبسائط العنصرية ومركباتها جوهراً مجرداً من عالم العقول يدبر أمره، حتى إن الذي لنوع النار هو الذي يحفظها وينورها، ويجذب الدهن والشمع إليها، ويسمونه رب النوع، ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع بملك الجبال وملك البحار ونحو ذلك ومع الاعتراف بكونه جزئياً يقولون. إنه كلي ذلك النوع، بمعنى أن نسبة فيضه إلى جميع على السواء، لا بمعنى أنه مشترك بينها حتى يلزم أن تكون إنسانية مجردة، موجودة في الأعيان مشتركة بين جميع الأفراد محققة في المواد، فيكون هناك إنسان محسوس فاسد آخر معقول مجرد دائم لا يتغير أبداً. ثم للمثل المعلقة التي يسمونها عالم الأشباح المجردة، فإنها لا تكون من الجواهر المجردة، بل كالواسطة بين المحسوس والمعقول، ولا تختص بأنواع الأجسام. بل يكون لكل شخص من الجواهر والأعراض على ما صرح بذلك صاحب الاشراق. فقال: والصور المعلقة ليست مثل أفلاطون. لأن مثل أفلاطون نورية . أي من عالم العقل، وهذه مثل مغلقة من عالم الأشباح المجردة منها ظلمانية، ومنها مستنيرة، وذكر أن لكل نوع من الفلكيات والعنصريات التي في عالم المثل أيضاً، رب نوع من عالم العقول، وأن رب النوع إنما يكون للأنواع الجسمانية المستقلة، وتدبير الأعراض والأجزاء مفوض إلى رب النوع الذي هو محلها من الأجسام. مثلاً في عالم العقل . جوهر مجرد له هيئات نورية، إذا ظله وقع في هذا العالم يكون منه المسك رائحته ، أو السكر مع طعمه، أو الإنسان مع اختلاف أعضائه.
And since this is clearly false, based on the fact that that which is receptive of opposites, and the part of individuals, is necessarily characterized by accidents, and that it is the essence (al-mahiyya) without condition (la bi-shart shay') and the essence with the condition of nothing (bi-shart la shay'), and that existence (al-wujud) is among the accidents. The Unconditioned Essence: Definition and the Objection of the Natural Universal
Defines the unconditioned essence and answers the natural universal objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the unconditioned essence (la bi-shart shay') is defined, why it exists externally as itself, and how it differs from the natural universal.

Core Idea
The unconditioned essence exists externally as itself; the natural universal is a mental condition, not the subject.

This lesson examines the third division of essence in kalam ontology: the unconditioned consideration (la bi-shart shay'). The author defines it, then addresses a key objection—that this unconditioned essence is merely a natural universal and cannot exist externally. Pay close attention to the distinction between the subject and the condition of universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its English translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the structure: definition, objection, answer.
2
Identify the definition of the unconditioned
Locate where the author defines the unconditioned and explains why it exists externally as the essence itself, not as a part.
The key phrase: 'فتوجد لكونها نفسها في الخارج لا جزءاً منها'.
3
Study the objection and the response
Read the objection that the unconditioned is a natural universal and thus cannot exist externally. Then study the response distinguishing the unconditioned from the natural universal.
The answer emphasizes that the natural universal is taken 'بشرط كونه معروضاً للكلية'.
4
Summarize the distinction
In your own words, explain how the unconditioned essence differs from the natural universal, using the passage's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث لا يعتبر فيه خلط ولا تجريد. قال: وقد تؤخذ لا بشرط شيء وهي أعم من المخلوط، فتوجد لكونها نفسها في الخارج لا جزءاً منها، إذ لا تمايز في الخارج فضلاً عن الجزئية وإنما ذلك في الذهن . فإن قيل : المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء كلي طبيعي، فيمتنع وجوده العيني، ضرورة استلزامه التشخص المنافي للكلية . قلنا : لا ، بل الكلي الطبيعي هو المأخوذ بشرط كونه معروضاً للكلية أنه موجود فمعناه أن معروضه الذي هو المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء
The third division: neither mixing nor abstraction is considered. He said: And it may be taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay'), and it is more general than the mixed. So it exists because it is itself in the external world, not as a part of it, for there is no distinction in the external world, let alone partiality, and that is only in the mind. For if it is said: That which is taken without condition is a natural universal, so its external existence is impossible, necessarily requiring individuation which contradicts universality. We say: No, rather the natural universal is that which is taken with the condition of being a subject of universality. That it exists means that its subject, which is that taken without condition, [exists].
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of essence: the unconditioned (la bi-shart shay'), which is more general than the mixed (al-makhlut). It exists externally as the very essence itself, not as a part, because in the external world there is no distinction between the essence and its parts—that only occurs in the mind. An objection is raised: the unconditioned is a natural universal, and thus it cannot exist externally because external existence requires individuation (tashakhkhus), which contradicts universality (kulliyya). The answer clarifies: the natural universal is that which is taken with the condition of being a subject of universality (bi-shart kawnihi ma'rudan li-l-kulliyya), whereas the unconditioned is the subject itself. In other words, the unconditioned essence is what serves as the subject for universality, and it is that which exists externally, while universality is a mental consideration added to it. Therefore, the objection confuses the unconditioned essence (which exists) with the natural universal (which is a mental consideration).

Key Terms
لا بشرط شيء
الكلي الطبيعي
المخلوط
التجريد
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unconditioned essence (la bi-shart shay') is the natural universal and thus cannot exist externally.
Correction: The unconditioned essence is the subject that exists externally; the natural universal is that subject considered with the condition of universality, which is a mental addition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the unconditioned essence (la bi-shart shay') exists externally?
A) Because it is taken with a condition of universality.
B) Because it is itself in the external world, not as a part, since there is no distinction in the external world.
C) Because it is abstracted from mixing and thus can be universal.
D) Because it is more general than the mixed and thus must exist.

Key Takeaway
The unconditioned essence exists externally as the subject, while the natural universal is a mental condition added to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often define yourself by conditions—roles, achievements, or faults—rather than seeing your unconditioned essence that exists as you before Allah? How would your life change if you focused on that core?
?
The passage says there is no distinction in the external world; distinctions only exist in the mind. When you judge others, do you mentally split them into parts and label them, forgetting they are whole individuals in reality? What can you do today to see someone without condition?
?
If the natural universal is a mental condition, then your concepts about people or yourself are not the full reality. What is one category you apply to yourself that might be limiting you from your true unconditioned self? Will you commit to looking beyond it?
