The Three Rational Judgments: Necessity, Impossibility, and Possibility
Al-Taftazani defines and exemplifies the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (necessity, impossibility, possibility) and how they arise intuitively from relating a concept to its existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are intuitive and foundational for all theological reasoning.

Al-Taftazani begins his theological investigations by establishing the most basic rational categories. Before any argument about God's attributes or actions can proceed, we must agree on what it means for something to be necessary, impossible, or possible. This passage defines these three judgments and shows that they are not learned but inherently known to every rational person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the passage defines the three judgments and then explains their origin.
2
Identify the three judgments
From the passage, identify the three rational judgments: necessity (وجوب), impossibility (امتناع), and possibility (إمكان). Note the examples given for each.
The examples are 'The Creator exists' (necessary), 'The conjunction of two opposites exists' (impossible), and 'Humans exist' (possible).
3
Understand their derivation
The passage explains that these judgments arise from relating a concept to a 'whetherness' (هلية), either simple or composite. This relation produces the attribution of existence or connection, which can be necessary, impossible, or possible.
4
Reflect on the necessary conception
Note that the author says the conception of these three is 'necessary' (ضروري), meaning it is intuitive and not acquired. The footnote warns that someone who does not distinguish them is not rational.
This is a key point: these judgments are a priori.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
هي معقولات تحصل من نسبة المفهوم إلى هلية بسيطة أو مركبة إذ حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته قد يجب، وقد يمتنع، وقد يمكن وتصورها ضروري (١). والتعريف بمثل ضرورة الوجود، وضرورة العدم، ولا ضرورتهما لفظي (٢). قد تقرر في موضعه أن (هل) إما بسيطة يطلب بها وجود الشيء في نفسه أو مركبة يطلب بها وجود شيء لشيء، فإذا نسب المفهوم إلى وجوده في نفسه، أو وجوده لأمر حصل في العقل معان هي الوجوب، والامتناع والإمكان، لأن حمل الوجود على الشيء أو ربط الشيء بالشيء بواسطته. قد في قولنا الباري تعالى موجود والأربعة توجد لها الزوجية، وقد كما في قولنا: اجتماع النقيضين موجود والأربعة توجد لها الفردية وقد يمكن كما في قولنا الإنسان موجود أو يوجد له الكتابة، ولاخفاء في حصولها عند حمل العدم، إذ الربط بواسطته، لكنه مندرج فيما ذكرنا، من حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته، لكونه أعم من الإيجابي والسلبي، وتصورات
(١) لحصوله لمن لا يمارس طرق الاكتساب، ولهذا يقال من لم يميز في نفسه بين الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز خرج عن طور العقلاء.
(٢) لفظي: يفيد أن ذلك المعنى المعروف الذي هو ضرورة الوجود هو المسمى بلفظ الوجوب، والمعنى المعروف الذي هو ضرورة العدم هو المسمى بلفظ الاستحالة، والمعنى المعروف الذي هو عدم ضرورة أحدهما هو المسمى بلفظ الجائز.
The First Investigation: They are intelligibles that arise from relating a concept to a simple or composite 'whetherness' (هلية), for the attribution of existence or the connection by means of it may be necessary, impossible, or possible, and their conception is necessary (١). And the definition by like necessity of existence, necessity of non-existence, and the lack of their necessity is verbal (٢). It has been established in its place that 'هل' is either simple, by which the existence of a thing in itself is sought, or composite, by which the existence of a thing for a thing is sought. So if the concept is related to its existence in itself, or its existence for something, meanings are obtained in the mind, which are necessity, impossibility, and possibility, because the attribution of existence to a thing or the connection of a thing to a thing by means of it. [It is] necessary in our statement: 'The Exalted Creator exists' and 'The number four has evenness'; impossible as in our statement: 'The conjunction of two opposites exists' and 'The number four has oddness'; and possible as in our statement: 'Humans exist' or 'Writing exists for them'. And there is no hiddenness in their occurrence when attributing non-existence, for the connection is by means of it, but it is included in what we mentioned, of the attribution of existence or the connection by means of it, because it is more general than positive and negative. And the conceptions...
(١) Because it occurs to one who does not practice the methods of acquisition; and for this reason it is said: Whoever does not distinguish in himself between necessity, impossibility, and possibility has left the realm of rational people.
(٢) Verbal: it indicates that the known meaning which is the necessity of existence is named by the word 'necessity', and the known meaning which is the necessity of non-existence is named by the word 'impossibility', and the known meaning which is the lack of necessity of either is named by the word 'possible'.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three rational judgments (المعقولات) that are foundational for kalam reasoning. It begins by stating that these intelligibles (معقولات) arise from relating a concept (نسبة المفهوم) to a 'whetherness' (هلية), which is either simple (بسيطة) or composite (مركبة). The simple 'هل' asks about the existence of a thing in itself, while the composite asks about the existence of a thing for another thing. When this relation occurs, the attribution of existence or the connection (الربط) by means of it may be necessary (يجب), impossible (يمتنع), or possible (يمكن). The author emphasizes that the conception (تصور) of these three is necessary (ضروري), meaning it is intuitive and occurs even to those untrained in logic. The footnote clarifies that someone who does not inherently distinguish these three is outside the realm of rational people. The definition using phrases like 'necessity of existence' etc. is merely verbal (لفظي)—it simply assigns names to pre-existing concepts. The passage then elaborates: when a concept is related to its existence in itself or for something, the mind obtains the three meanings (necessity, impossibility, possibility). Examples are given: 'The Creator exists' and 'Four has evenness' are necessary; 'The conjunction of two opposites exists' and 'Four has oddness' are impossible; 'Humans exist' or 'Writing exists for them' are possible. The same applies to attributing non-existence, as it is included under the general notion of attribution. The text ends with 'وتصورات' suggesting continuation, but the core definition and explanation are complete.

معقولات
هلية
وجوب
امتناع
إمكان
ضروري
لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three rational judgments are derived from empirical observation or acquired through learning.
Correction: The author explicitly states that their conception is necessary (ضروري), meaning intuitive and present even without practice. The footnote reinforces that anyone who does not naturally distinguish them is outside the realm of rational people.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments arise a priori from relating a concept to existence; they are intuitive and not learned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the statement 'God exists', do you feel the weight of its necessity in your heart as an intuitive certainty, or has it become a mere recitation? How would your life change if you truly distinguished this necessity from mere possibility?
?
The passage warns that anyone who does not distinguish between necessity, impossibility, and possibility has 'left the realm of rational people'. In what area of your belief or behavior have you confused the possible with the necessary, treating something that is not essential as if it were, or vice versa?
?
If the conception of these judgments is necessary and intuitive, why do you sometimes find yourself hesitating or doubting in matters of creed? Is it because you have allowed acquired opinions to cloud your primordial rational judgment? What concrete step can you take today to purify your understanding of these fundamental categories?

The Origin of the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi introduces the three rational judgments and their derivation from the simple/compound 'hal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments of necessity, impossibility, and possibility arise from relating a concept to existence via the simple or compound 'hal'.

Core Idea
The three judgments spring from our mind's relation of a concept to its existence.

Sanusi begins by explaining the foundational source of the three rational judgments. He shows that they are not arbitrary but derived from the basic act of considering whether something exists, either in itself or for something else. Understanding this origin is key to grasping their logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the origin of judgments
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Note the definition of 'hal' (simple and compound) and how it leads to the three judgments.
Pay attention to the examples: God exists (necessary), conjunction of opposites exists (impossible), man exists (possible).
2
Understand the two types of 'hal'
The simple 'hal' asks about existence in itself, the compound asks about existence for something. This distinction is crucial for the classification of judgments.
3
Identify the three judgments in the examples
Notice how the attribution of existence can be necessary, impossible, or possible. These are the three rational judgments that form the basis of all theological reasoning.
Also note that the same applies to non-existence, but it is included under the positive attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : هي معقولات تحصل من نسبة المفهوم، إلى هليــة البسيطة أو المركبة إذ حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته قد يجب، وقد يمتنع ، وقد يمكن وتصورها ضروري . والتعريف بمثل ضرورة الوجود، وضرورة العدم، ولا ضرورتهما لفظي . قد تقرر في موضعه أن (هل) إما بسيطة يطلب بها وجود الشيء في نفسه أو مركبة يطلب بها وجود شيء لشيء، فإذا نسب المفهوم إلى وجوده في نفسه، أو وجوده لأمر حصل في العقل معان هي الوجوب، والامتناع والإمكان ، لأن حمل الوجود على الشيء أو ربط الشيء بالشيء بواسطته. قد في قولنا الباري تعالى موجود والأربعة توجد لها الزوجية، وقد كما في قولنا : اجتماع النقيضين موجود والأربعة توجد لها الفردية وقد يمكن كما في قولنا الإنسان موجود أو يوجد له الكتابة، ولاخفاء في حصولها عند حمل العدم، إذ الربط بواسطته، لكنه مندرج فيما ذكرنا، من حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته، لكونه أعم من الإيجابي والسلبي، وتصورات هذه المعاني ضرورية حاصلة لمن لم يمارس طرق الاكتساب إلا أنها قد تعرف تعريفات لفظية كالوجود والعدم . فيقال: الوجوب ضرورة الوجود أو اقتضاؤه أو استحالته العدم، والامتناع ضرورة العدم أو اقتضاؤه، أو استحالة الوجود، والإمكان جواز الوجود والعدم، أو عدم ضرورتهما، أو عدم اقتضاء شيء منهما ، ولهذا لا يتحاشى عن أن يقال الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن عدمه والممتنع ما يجب عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن وجوده والممكن ما لا يجب وجوده، ولا عدمه أو ما لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه، ولو كان القصد إلى إفادة تصور هذه المعاني، لكان دوراً ظاهراً، وظهر هذه المفهومات الوجود لكونه تأكد الوجود، الذي هو أعرف من العدم، لما أنه يعرف بذاته، والعدم يعرف بوجه ما ،بالوجود والنزاع في أن مفهوم الوجوب والإمكان وجودي أو عدمي، مبني على اختلاف مفهومات الخواص، التي باعتبارها يطلقان على الواجب والممكن. وأما في الواجب فكاقتضاء الوجود بحسب والاستغناء عن الغير، وعدم التوقف عليه، وما به يمتاز الواجب عن الممكن والممتنع ، وأما في الممكن ؛ فالاحتياج إلى الغير والتوقف عليه وعدم الاستغناء عنه، وعدم اقتضاء الوجود أو العدم، أو ما به يمتاز الممكن عن الواجب والممتنع .
The first topic: They are rational judgments that arise from the relation of a concept to the simple or compound 'hal', for the attribution of existence or the connection by means of it is sometimes necessary, sometimes impossible, and sometimes possible, and their conception is necessary. And definition by something like necessity of existence, necessity of non-existence, and non-necessity of both is verbal. It has been established in its place that 'hal' is either simple, by which the existence of a thing in itself is sought, or compound, by which the existence of a thing for a thing is sought. So when the concept is related to its existence in itself, or its existence for something, meanings arise in the mind which are necessity, impossibility, and possibility, because the attribution of existence to a thing or the connection of a thing to a thing by means of it: it may be necessary as in our saying 'The Exalted Creator exists' and 'Four has evenness for it'; it may be impossible as in our saying 'The conjunction of opposites exists' and 'Four has oddness for it'; and it may be possible as in our saying 'Man exists' or 'Writing exists for him'. And there is no concealment of their occurrence when attributing non-existence, for the connection is by means of it, but it is included in what we mentioned, of attributing existence or the connection by means of it, because it is more general than the positive and the negative. And the conceptions of these meanings are necessary, occurring to one who does not practice the methods of acquisition, except that they may be known by verbal definitions like existence and non-existence. So it is said: Necessity is the necessity of existence or its requirement or the impossibility of non-existence; impossibility is the necessity of non-existence or its requirement or the impossibility of existence; possibility is the permissibility of existence and non-existence, or the non-necessity of both, or the non-requirement of either of them. And for this reason one does not refrain from saying: The necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, or that whose non-existence is not possible; the impossible is that whose non-existence is necessary, or that whose existence is not possible; the possible is that whose existence is not necessary nor its non-existence, or that whose existence is not impossible nor its non-existence. And if the intention were to convey the conception of these meanings, it would be an obvious circle. And the most evident of these concepts is existence, because it is the intensification of existence, which is more known than non-existence, since it is known by itself, while non-existence is known by some aspect through existence. And the dispute over whether the concept of necessity and possibility is positive or negative is based on the difference in the concepts of specific properties, by virtue of which they are applied to the necessary and the possible. As for the necessary, it is like the requirement of existence according to essence and independence from others and non-dependence on it, and that by which the necessary is distinguished from the possible and the impossible. As for the possible, it is need for others and dependence on it and non-independence from it, and non-requirement of existence or non-existence, or that by which the possible is distinguished from the necessary and the impossible.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the three rational judgments (المعقولات) that are foundational in kalam and logic: necessity (الوجوب), impossibility (الامتناع), and possibility (الإمكان). The author begins by stating that these judgments arise from relating a concept to the simple or compound 'hal' (هل). 'Hal' refers to the interrogative particle used to ask about existence. The simple 'hal' asks about the existence of a thing in itself, while the compound 'hal' asks about the existence of a thing for another thing. When we relate a concept to its existence—either in itself or for something—three mental meanings are produced: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. This is because the attribution of existence (or the connection by means of existence) can be necessary (e.g., 'God exists', 'Four has evenness'), impossible (e.g., 'The conjunction of opposites exists', 'Four has oddness'), or possible (e.g., 'Man exists', 'Man has writing'). The same applies when attributing non-existence, but it is included under the general notion of attribution of existence or connection, because existence is more general and includes both positive and negative attributions.

The author then notes that these concepts are necessary (ضرورية), meaning they are innate and known to everyone without training. However, they can be defined verbally (لفظي), by terms like necessity of existence, etc. He gives several verbal definitions: necessity is the necessity of existence or its requirement or the impossibility of non-existence; impossibility is the necessity of non-existence or its requirement or the impossibility of existence; possibility is the permissibility of existence and non-existence, or the non-necessity of both, or the non-requirement of either. Consequently, one can also define the subjects: the necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible; the impossible is that whose existence is impossible; the possible is that whose existence is not necessary nor its non-existence. He warns that if these definitions were meant to convey the original conception, they would be circular, but they are merely verbal clarifications.

The author then observes that the clearest of these concepts is existence, because it is more known than non-existence—existence is known by itself, while non-existence is known through existence. Finally, he addresses the debate about whether the concepts of necessity and possibility are positive (وجودي) or negative (عدمي). This debate arises from differing understandings of the specific properties (خواص) that distinguish the necessary from the possible. For the necessary, these properties include requiring existence by essence, independence from others, and non-dependence; for the possible, they include need for others, dependence, and non-requirement of existence or non-existence. Thus, the concepts may be viewed as positive (as properties) or negative (as privations), but the author presents both sides without taking a definitive stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you sometimes fall into the illusion that your existence is necessary, forgetting that you are contingent? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that you exist only by the will of the Necessary Being?
?
The passage says these judgments are innate and 'their conception is necessary'. Is your belief in the necessity of God's existence merely a verbal affirmation, or has it penetrated your heart such that you live with constant awareness of His necessary attributes? How would your fear and hope be transformed if you truly felt that God's existence is necessary and your own is entirely dependent on Him?
?
Sanusi includes the example 'Writing exists for him' under possibility. Which skills or traits do you currently treat as if they were necessary for your identity or worth, when they are in fact merely possible? Are you willing to let go of that attachment this week and rely on the One whose existence alone is necessary?

The Innate Nature and Verbal Definitions of the Judgments
Sanusi discusses the innate nature of the three judgments and provides verbal definitions, warning against circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three rational judgments are innate yet definable verbally, and that verbal definitions are not circular in a harmful sense.

Core Idea
These judgments are innate, but we can clarify them with verbal definitions.

In this section, Sanusi emphasizes that the conceptions of necessity, impossibility, and possibility are necessary (innate) and known to everyone, even without learning. However, they can be defined verbally by terms like 'necessity of existence' etc. He warns that if these definitions were meant to convey the original conception, they would be circular, but they are merely terminological clarifications. This distinction is important for avoiding misunderstanding about the role of definitions in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on innate nature and definitions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the claim that these concepts are necessary and known even to those untrained.
The term 'necessary' here means self-evident, not acquired.
2
Examine the verbal definitions given
Sanusi provides several verbal definitions: necessity as necessity of existence, impossibility as necessity of non-existence, possibility as permissibility of both. See how they are interchangeable.
These definitions are not meant to teach new concepts but to clarify terminology.
3
Understand the circularity warning
If these definitions were intended to convey the primary conception, they would be circular because they presuppose the concepts. But they are only for terminological precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : هي معقولات تحصل من نسبة المفهوم، إلى هليــة البسيطة أو المركبة إذ حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته قد يجب، وقد يمتنع ، وقد يمكن وتصورها ضروري . والتعريف بمثل ضرورة الوجود، وضرورة العدم، ولا ضرورتهما لفظي . قد تقرر في موضعه أن (هل) إما بسيطة يطلب بها وجود الشيء في نفسه أو مركبة يطلب بها وجود شيء لشيء، فإذا نسب المفهوم إلى وجوده في نفسه، أو وجوده لأمر حصل في العقل معان هي الوجوب، والامتناع والإمكان ، لأن حمل الوجود على الشيء أو ربط الشيء بالشيء بواسطته. قد في قولنا الباري تعالى موجود والأربعة توجد لها الزوجية، وقد كما في قولنا : اجتماع النقيضين موجود والأربعة توجد لها الفردية وقد يمكن كما في قولنا الإنسان موجود أو يوجد له الكتابة، ولاخفاء في حصولها عند حمل العدم، إذ الربط بواسطته، لكنه مندرج فيما ذكرنا، من حمل الوجود أو الربط بواسطته، لكونه أعم من الإيجابي والسلبي، وتصورات هذه المعاني ضرورية حاصلة لمن لم يمارس طرق الاكتساب إلا أنها قد تعرف تعريفات لفظية كالوجود والعدم . فيقال: الوجوب ضرورة الوجود أو اقتضاؤه أو استحالته العدم، والامتناع ضرورة العدم أو اقتضاؤه، أو استحالة الوجود، والإمكان جواز الوجود والعدم، أو عدم ضرورتهما، أو عدم اقتضاء شيء منهما ، ولهذا لا يتحاشى عن أن يقال الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن عدمه والممتنع ما يجب عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن وجوده والممكن ما لا يجب وجوده، ولا عدمه أو ما لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه، ولو كان القصد إلى إفادة تصور هذه المعاني، لكان دوراً ظاهراً، وظهر هذه المفهومات الوجود لكونه تأكد الوجود، الذي هو أعرف من العدم، لما أنه يعرف بذاته، والعدم يعرف بوجه ما ،بالوجود والنزاع في أن مفهوم الوجوب والإمكان وجودي أو عدمي، مبني على اختلاف مفهومات الخواص، التي باعتبارها يطلقان على الواجب والممكن. وأما في الواجب فكاقتضاء الوجود بحسب والاستغناء عن الغير، وعدم التوقف عليه، وما به يمتاز الواجب عن الممكن والممتنع ، وأما في الممكن ؛ فالاحتياج إلى الغير والتوقف عليه وعدم الاستغناء عنه، وعدم اقتضاء الوجود أو العدم، أو ما به يمتاز الممكن عن الواجب والممتنع .
The first topic: They are rational judgments that arise from the relation of a concept to the simple or compound 'hal', for the attribution of existence or the connection by means of it is sometimes necessary, sometimes impossible, and sometimes possible, and their conception is necessary. And for this reason one does not refrain from saying: The necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, or that whose non-existence is not possible; the impossible is that whose non-existence is necessary, or that whose existence is not possible; the possible is that whose existence is not necessary nor its non-existence, or that whose existence is not impossible nor its non-existence. The Primacy of Existence and the Positive/Negative Debate
Sanusi discusses the primacy of existence and the dispute over whether necessity and possibility are positive or negative concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is the most evident concept and why there is a debate about whether necessity and possibility are positive or negative, based on distinguishing properties.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident, but necessity and possibility may be viewed as positive or negative depending on properties.

In the final part of this passage, Sanusi observes that existence is the clearest of these concepts because it is known by itself, while non-existence is known through existence. He then introduces a scholarly dispute about whether the concepts of necessity and possibility are positive (existent) or negative (privative). This debate hinges on the specific properties (خواص) that distinguish the necessary from the possible. For the necessary, properties include requiring existence by essence and independence; for the possible, need and dependence. Thus, these concepts can be interpreted either way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on primacy of existence
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that existence is more evident than non-existence.
Existence is self-evident; non-existence is understood through existence.
2
Understand the dispute on positive/negative
The dispute is based on the different properties (خواص) that are used to define the necessary and the possible. These properties can be considered positive (like requiring existence) or negative (like non-dependence).
3
Examine the properties of the necessary
The necessary has properties like requiring existence by essence, independence from others, and non-dependence. These can be seen as positive attributes.
4
Examine the properties of the possible
The possible has properties like need for others, dependence, and non-requirement of existence or non-existence. These can be seen as negative or positive depending on perspective.

Correction: The passage shows that they can be understood positively through their distinguishing properties, though a dispute exists.

Existence is self-evident, and whether necessity and possibility are positive or negative depends on which distinguishing properties one emphasizes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Existence is said to be 'more known' than non-existence, yet you often think of things by what they are not. When doubt whispers that God's existence is not proven, are you letting non-existence define your view rather than existence itself? Will you affirm existence first, and then reason from there?
?
The passage shows that your dependence on others (a property of possibility) can be seen negatively as need or positively as a distinguishing feature. In your relationship with Allah, do you secretly resent your dependence, or do you embrace it as the very mark of a creature? How would your worship change if you saw your need as a sign of your true nature?
?
Sanusi presents a debate without taking sides. Are you comfortable with theological ambiguity, or do you demand clear-cut answers? Could your impatience with unresolved questions hide a pride that thinks it must know everything? Will you humbly study debates you cannot settle, trusting that wisdom lies in the process?

The Three Modal Judgments: Definitions Based on Existence and Non-Existence
Sanusi enumerates the three modal judgments and provides their verbal definitions using existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments (necessity, impossibility, possibility) are defined in terms of existence and non-existence, and why these definitions are merely verbal.

Core Idea
The three modal judgments are self-evident but can be clarified through existence and non-existence.

This lesson introduces the foundational categories of Islamic theology: the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. The author explains that although these concepts are innate, they can be defined verbally by referring to existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam). You will see multiple formulations and learn why these definitions are not intended to create new concepts but to clarify what is already known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses multiple equivalent definitions.
2
Identify the three definitions
The passage presents definitions for necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). Find each one and notice how each is expressed in terms of existence and non-existence.
3
Understand the interchangeable expressions
The author shows that one can also define the necessary as 'that whose non-existence is impossible' etc. Notice the equivalence between the formulations.
This shows the flexibility of verbal definitions.
4
Grasp the warning about circularity
The author warns that if these definitions were intended to produce conceptualization (tasawwur), they would be circular. Reflect on why that would be the case.
The definitions rely on the very concepts they are trying to define.
5
Reflect on the epistemological priority of existence
The author states that existence is better known than non-existence. Consider why existence is considered self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه المعاني ضرورية حاصلة لمن لم يمارس طرق الاكتساب إلا أنها قد تعرف تعريفات لفظية كالوجود والعدم. فيقال: الوجوب ضرورة الوجود أو اقتضاؤه أو استحالته العدم، والامتناع ضرورة العدم أو اقتضاؤه، أو استحالة الوجود، والإمكان جواز الوجود والعدم، أو عدم ضرورتهما، أو عدم اقتضاء شيء منهما، ولهذا لا يتحاشى عن أن يقال الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن عدمه والممتنع ما يجب عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن وجوده والممكن ما لا يجب وجوده، ولا عدمه أو ما لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه، ولو كان القصد إلى إفادة تصور هذه المعاني، لكان دوراً ظاهراً، وظهر هذه المفهومات الوجود لكونه تأكد الوجود، الذي هو أعرف من العدم، لما أنه يعرف بذاته، والعدم يعرف بوجه ما بالوجود والنزاع في أن مفهوم الوجوب والإمكان وجودي أو عدمي، مبني على اختلاف مفهومات الخواص، التي باعتبارها يطلقان على الواجب والممكن. وأما في الواجب فكاقتضاء الوجود بحسب والاستغناء عن الغير، وعدم التوقف عليه، وما به يمتاز الواجب عن الممكن والممتنع، وأما في الممكن؛ فالاحتياج إلى الغير والتوقف عليه وعدم الاستغناء عنه، وعدم اقتضاء الوجود أو العدم، أو ما به يمتاز الممكن عن الواجب والممتنع.
These concepts are necessary (self-evident) for one who has not practiced the methods of acquisition, except that they may be known by verbal definitions like existence and non-existence. So it is said: Necessity is the necessity of existence or its requirement or the impossibility of non-existence; Impossibility is the necessity of non-existence or its requirement, or the impossibility of existence; Possibility is the permissibility of existence and non-existence, or the lack of necessity of both, or the lack of requirement of either. For this reason one does not refrain from saying: The necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, or that whose non-existence is not possible; the impossible is that whose existence is necessary to not be, or that whose existence is not possible; the possible is that whose existence is not necessary, nor its non-existence, or that whose existence and non-existence are not impossible. And if the intention were to convey the conceptualization of these meanings, it would be an obvious circularity. And the appearance of these concepts is from existence because existence is more known than non-existence, since it is known by itself, while non-existence is known in some way by existence. And the dispute over whether the concept of necessity and possibility is positive or negative is based on differing concepts of the properties by which they are applied to the necessary and the possible. As for the necessary, it is like requiring existence with respect to essence and independence from others, and not depending on it, and that by which the necessary is distinguished from the possible and impossible. As for the possible: it is needing others and depending on them, and not being independent of them, and not requiring existence or non-existence, or that by which the possible is distinguished from the necessary and impossible.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage defines the three modal judgments: necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). It begins by stating that these concepts are self-evident (daruriyya) to anyone, even without formal study, but they can be described through verbal definitions involving existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam). The author then provides multiple equivalent formulations for each modal. For necessity, it is the necessity of existence or its requirement or the impossibility of non-existence. For impossibility, it is the necessity of non-existence or its requirement or the impossibility of existence. For possibility, it is the permissibility of both existence and non-existence, or the absence of necessity of either, or the absence of requirement of either. He notes that because these concepts are clear, one can interchangeably define the necessary as 'that whose non-existence is impossible' and so on. However, if these definitions were intended to produce the very conceptualization (tasawwur) of the concepts, they would be circular, since they rely on the same notions. The author then explains that these concepts are ultimately understood from existence, which is known directly (by itself), while non-existence is known indirectly through existence. He then addresses a dispute: whether the concepts of necessity and possibility are positive (wujudi) or negative ('adami). This controversy stems from differences in the specific properties (khawass) by which these judgments are applied. For the necessary, the property is requiring existence, independence from others, and not depending on anything else—features that distinguish it from the possible and impossible. For the possible, the property is needing others, depending on them, and not requiring either existence or non-existence—features that distinguish it from the necessary and impossible. Thus the passage clarifies both the self-evident nature of modal concepts and the terminological variability in defining them.

دور
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verbal definitions are intended to create new conceptual understanding of necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
Correction: The author emphasizes that these concepts are already self-evident; the definitions are merely verbal clarifications. If they were meant to produce the concepts themselves, they would be circular.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would it be circular to say these definitions produce the conceptualization of the modal concepts?
A) Because the concepts are self-evident.
B) Because the definitions rely on existence and non-existence.
C) Because the definitions themselves use the same concepts (e.g., necessity, impossibility).
D) Because existence is better known than non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The three modal judgments are self-evident concepts that can be verbally defined through existence and non-existence, but these definitions are not meant to generate conceptual understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'God is necessary,' do you merely repeat a verbal definition, or do you truly grasp what it means for His non-existence to be impossible? This passage shows that verbal definitions can be circular if they are not grounded in self-evident understanding. Examine your heart: do you know Allah's necessity directly, or do you only recite it? Ask yourself today to move from verbal acknowledgment to inward certainty.
?
Existence is known by itself, says Sanusi. Do you experience Allah's existence as directly as your own? If not, what distractions veil that immediate knowledge? Commit to spending time in silence, beyond definitions, to sense the sheer necessity of His presence.
?
The passage distinguishes between verbal definition and true conceptualization. Where in your own faith is there a gap between what you can say and what you truly understand? Pick one Islamic concept (e.g., tawheed, risala, akhira) and examine whether your understanding is merely borrowed from others or truly self-evident to you. Resolve to deepen your grasp through reflection, not just repetition.

The Dispute Over the Positive or Negative Nature of Necessity and Possibility
Sanusi explains the dispute over whether necessity and possibility are positive or negative concepts and lists the defining properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis of the scholarly dispute about whether the concepts of necessity and possibility are positive (existence-affirming) or negative (non-existence-affirming), and how the properties (khawass) of the necessary and possible determine their application.

Core Idea
Whether necessity is a positive or negative concept depends on the property used to define it.

In this lesson, we explore a subtle debate in Islamic theology: are the concepts of necessity (wujub) and possibility (imkan) positive (wujudi) or negative ('adami) in nature? The author explains that this controversy arises from differing interpretations of the defining properties (khawass) of the necessary and the possible. You will learn the properties associated with each category and how they resolve the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the dispute
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the dispute and the properties listed.
This passage is more advanced.
2
Identify the two positions
Some say necessity and possibility are positive concepts (wujudi), others say they are negative ('adami). The dispute is based on different interpretations of the properties.
3
Understand the role of properties (khawass)
The dispute is built upon differing concepts of the properties by which these judgments are applied. The properties define what it means for something to be necessary or possible.
4
Learn the properties of the necessary
The necessary is characterized by requiring existence with respect to essence, independence from others, and not depending on anything.
These properties distinguish it from the possible and impossible.
5
Learn the properties of the possible
The possible is characterized by needing others, depending on them, not being independent, and not requiring existence or non-existence.

Essential and Accidental Modal Judgments
Classification of modal judgments into essential and accidental with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three modal judgments (necessity, impossibility, possibility) are divided into essential and accidental categories, and the four types of accidental judgments.

Core Idea
Modal judgments are either inherent to a thing's essence or depend on an external cause.

In this passage, the author classifies the three rational judgments of necessity, impossibility, and possibility based on whether they arise from the essence of a thing or from something else. This classification is foundational for evaluating theological arguments. Let's explore the distinction and the various types of accidental judgments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential vs. accidental
Understand that if a modal judgment is based on the thing's essence it is essential; otherwise it is accidental (and further classified).
The author uses the terms 'dhati' and 'ghayri'.
2
Examine essential examples
The passage gives examples like the existence of God (essential necessity), impossibility of opposites (essential impossibility), and evenness for four (essential necessity).
3
Identify four accidental subtypes
The author lists four ways a judgment can be accidental: based on the specific cause, description of the subject, time, or predication under a condition.
Each subtype has an example from the passage.
4
Reflect on importance
Distinguishing essential from accidental is crucial in kalām for precise reasoning about God's attributes and actions.
This classification helps avoid conflating inherent qualities with contingent ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في تقسيم الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان. كل من الواجب والامتناع والإمكان، إن كان بالنظر إلى ذات الشيء، فذاتي وإلا فغيري، أو وضعي، أو وقتي أو غيرها). قد يكون بالذات، وقد يكون بالغير، لأن ضرورة وجود الشيء، أو لا وجوده في نفسه أو ضرورة وجود شيء لشيء آخر، أو لا وجوده له إن كانت بالنظر إلى ذاته كوجود الباري، وعدم اجتماع النقيضين، ووجود الزوجية للأربعة وعدم الفردية لها، فذاتي وإلا فغيري، وهو وإن لم ينفك علة، لكن قد ينظر إلى خصوص العلة كوجوب الحركة للحجر المرمي، وامتناع السكون له وقد ينظر إلى وصف الذات الموضوع كوجوب حركة الأصابع للكاتب، وامتناع سكونها له، وقد ينظر إلى وقت له كوجوب الانخساف للقمر في وقت المقابلة المخصوصة، وامتناعه في وقت التربيع، وقد ينظر إلى ثبوت المحمول له كوجود الحركة للجسم المأخوذ، بشرط كونه متحركاً، وامتناع السكون له حينئذ.
The second investigation: on the division of necessity, impossibility, and possibility. Each of necessity, impossibility, and possibility: if it is with respect to the essence of the thing, then essential; otherwise accidental, or conditional, or temporal, or other. It may be essential, and it may be accidental, because the necessity of a thing's existence or non-existence in itself, or the necessity of a thing for another, or its non-existence for it, if it is with respect to its essence, like the existence of the Creator and the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites, and the existence of evenness for four and the non-existence of oddness for it, then essential; otherwise accidental. And although it is not separate from a cause, it may be with respect to the specific cause, like the necessity of motion for a thrown stone and the impossibility of rest for it; and it may be with respect to the description of the subject's essence, like the necessity of movement of the fingers for the writer and the impossibility of their rest for him; and it may be with respect to a time for it, like the necessity of the eclipse for the moon at the specific time of opposition, and its impossibility at the time of quadrature; and it may be with respect to the establishment of the predicate for it, like the existence of motion for the body taken under condition of being moving, and the impossibility of rest for it then.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents a classification (taqsim) of the three modal judgments: necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). The author first states a general rule: each of these, if considered with respect to the essence (dhat) of the thing, is essential (dhati); otherwise it is accidental (ghayri), or conditional (wad'i), or temporal (waqti), or other categories. He then elaborates on the essential vs. accidental distinction. The key principle is whether the necessity or impossibility arises from the thing itself or from something else. Essential judgments are those that are inherent to the essence, such as the existence of God (necessary in itself), the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites (impossible in itself), and the necessity of evenness for four and the impossibility of oddness for four. Accidental judgments are those that depend on an external cause. The author notes that accidental necessity/impossibility does not occur without a cause ('illa), and then enumerates four types of accidental judgments based on what the cause is considered in relation to: (1) with respect to the specific cause itself, e.g., the necessity of motion for a thrown stone due to the throw; (2) with respect to a description (wasf) of the subject's essence, e.g., the necessity of finger movement for a writer when described as writing; (3) with respect to a specific time, e.g., the necessity of the moon's eclipse at the time of opposition; (4) with respect to the predication (thubut al-mahmul) under a condition, e.g., the necessity of motion for a body when described as moving. In each case, the judgment is accidental because it depends on an external factor, not on the thing's essence alone. This classification shows the logical structure of modal statements in kalām argumentation.

Key Terms
ذاتي
غيري
وضعي
وقتي
علة
وصف الذات
ثبوت المحمول
تقسيم
وجوب
امتناع
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse essential necessity with accidental necessity, thinking that everything necessary is necessary in itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that many modal judgments are accidental and depend on external causes, not on the thing's essence alone.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is given in the passage as an example of essential impossibility?
A) Rest for a thrown stone
B) Movement of fingers for a writer
C) Conjunction of opposites
D) Eclipse of the moon at quadrature

Key Takeaway
The three modal judgments can be essential (based on essence) or accidental (dependent on external factors), and accidental judgments have four subtypes: cause, description, time, and predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only God is necessary in His essence, and everything else—including your very existence—is either impossible in itself or merely possible and dependent on Him, how does that truth confront the self-sufficiency you feel in your daily life? Will you consciously acknowledge your dependence by beginning each task with 'Bismillah' and seeking His aid?
?
This lesson shows that many of the necessities you experience (like your heartbeat or the sun rising) are accidental—they depend on external causes that God sustains. When you realize that these 'ordinary' necessities are not intrinsic but are gifts from Him moment by moment, does it not demand a response of constant gratitude? What specific habit will you adopt today to turn these unnoticed blessings into moments of thanks?
?
The classification of judgments includes those based on time or condition—your actions are necessary only under certain descriptions (e.g., as a writer). If you are a 'writer' only when you choose to write, then your identity and responsibilities are contingent on your choices. Will you take ownership of the roles you have been given (parent, student, employee) as conditions that make certain actions necessary for you—and thereby necessary for your accountability before Allah? What role will you recommit to fulfilling today?

Essential and Accidental Modalities in Kalam
Taftazani classifies modalities as essential or accidental with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments (necessity, impossibility, possibility) are classified as essential or accidental, and the four subtypes of accidental modality.

Core Idea
Modality is not always essential; external causes can make something necessary or impossible.

In kalam, the judgments of necessity, impossibility, and possibility do not always stem from the essence of a thing. Often they depend on external factors such as causes, descriptions, times, or conditions. In this passage, Taftazani lays out this classification with clear examples, showing how the same judgment can be essential in one context and accidental in another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main classification
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice that Taftazani first divides each modality into essential (dhati) and accidental (ghayri).
Essential judgments are those that belong to the thing by virtue of its own essence.
2
Examine the essential examples
Look at the examples of essential necessity (existence of the Creator), essential impossibility (conjunction of two contradictories), and essential possibility (evenness for four). Understand why these are essential.
These judgments do not depend on anything external.
3
Learn the four subtypes of accidental modality
Taftazani then lists four ways a judgment can be accidental: by specific cause, by description of the subject, by time, and by affirmation of a predicate under a condition. Study each subtype and its example.
Each subtype highlights a different external factor.
4
Contrast essential and accidental with your own examples
Try to think of another example for each category. This will deepen your understanding of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
في تقسيم الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان

المبحث الثاني: كل من الواجب والامتناع والإمكان، إن كان بالنظر إلى ذات الشيء، فذاتي وإلا فغيري، أو وضعي، أو وقتي أو غيرها.

قد يكون بالذات، وقد يكون بالغير، لأن ضرورة وجود الشيء، أو لا وجوده في نفسه أو ضرورة وجود شيء لشيء آخر، أو لا وجوده له إن كانت بالنظر إلى ذاته كوجود الباري، وعدم اجتماع النقيضين، ووجود الزوجية للأربعة وعدم الفردية لها، فذاتي وإلا فغيري، وهو وإن لم ينفك علة، لكن قد ينظر إلى خصوص العلة كوجوب الحركة للحجر المرمي، وامتناع السكون له، وقد ينظر إلى وصف الذات الموضوع كوجوب حركة الأصابع للكاتب، وامتناع سكونها له، وقد ينظر إلى وقت له كوجوب الانخساف للقمر في وقت المقابلة المخصوصة، وامتناعه في وقت التربيع، وقد ينظر إلى ثبوت المحمول له كوجود الحركة للجسم المأخوذ، بشرط كونه متحركاً، وامتناع السكون له حينئذ.
Second section: On the division of necessity, impossibility, and possibility. The second section: Each of the necessary, the impossible, and the possible — if it is with respect to the essence of the thing, then it is essential; otherwise it is accidental, or positional, or temporal, or other. It may be by essence, and it may be by another, because the necessity of a thing’s existence or its non-existence in itself, or the necessity of a thing’s existence for another thing, or its non-existence for it — if it is with respect to its essence, like the existence of the Creator and the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories, and the existence of evenness for four and the non-existence of oddness for it — then essential; otherwise accidental. And though it is not free of a cause, yet sometimes one considers the specific cause, like the necessity of motion for a thrown stone and the impossibility of rest for it; and sometimes one considers the description of the essence-subject, like the necessity of movement of the fingers for the writer and the impossibility of their rest for him; and sometimes one considers a time for it, like the necessity of eclipse for the moon at the time of the specific opposition, and its impossibility at the time of quadrature; and sometimes one considers the affirmation of the predicate for it, like the existence of motion for a body taken with the condition of its being moving, and the impossibility of rest for it then.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three modal judgments — necessity, impossibility, and possibility — into essential (dhati) and accidental (ghayri), with further subtypes. The author begins by stating that each of the three (wajib, mumtani', mumkin) can be considered either with respect to the essence of the thing (dhati) or not (ghayri). He then explains that necessity or impossibility can be by essence (e.g., the existence of God is necessary by essence, the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible by essence, evenness is necessary for four by essence). When the necessity or impossibility is due to something external, it is accidental. This accidental modality can be further divided: (1) by considering the specific cause (e.g., motion is necessary for a thrown stone due to the force), (2) by considering the description of the subject of the essence (e.g., movement of the writer's fingers is necessary given the description 'writing'), (3) by considering a specific time (e.g., lunar eclipse is necessary at opposition, impossible at quadrature), and (4) by considering the affirmation of a predicate under a condition (e.g., motion is necessary for a moving body). The author follows the order of the original definition, then expands each subtype with examples.

ممتنع
ممكن

غيري
وضعي
وقتي
علة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessity and impossibility are always essential and never depend on external factors.
Correction: This passage shows that modalities can be accidental, depending on causes, descriptions, times, or conditions, as illustrated by the examples.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of essential (dhati) impossibility?
A) Motion for a thrown stone
B) Evenness for four
C) Conjunction of two contradictories
D) Eclipse of the moon at quadrature

Key Takeaway
Modalities can be essential or accidental; accidental ones depend on external factors such as cause, description, time, or condition.

عندما تنظر إلى أسباب نجاحك، هل تعزوها إلى ذاتك أم إلى الله ثم الأسباب؟ كيف يمكن لهذا التصنيف (ذاتي/غيري) أن يصحح نظرتك للنعم؟

Existence as Predicate vs. Copula
Al-Taftazani explains the difference between existence as predicate and copula, resolving an objection from al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between existence used as a predicate and existence used as a copula, and how this resolves an objection about necessary concomitants being necessary in themselves.

Core Idea
Existence can function as a predicate or a copula, changing what 'necessary' means.

This passage from al-Taftazani addresses a subtle but crucial point in Islamic logic and theology: the two ways existence can be used in a proposition. Understanding this distinction clarifies how necessary concomitants of quiddities (like evenness for four) are necessary but not necessary in themselves. The passage also refutes a potential objection from al-Mawaqif.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structures: 'if existence is taken as a predicate... if taken as a copula'.
2
Identify the two uses of existence
The passage presents two cases: existence as predicate (mahmul) and existence as copula (rabita). For each, note what 'wajib al-wujud' means. Write down the key difference in your own words.
When existence is a predicate, it describes the subject itself. When it is a copula, it links subject and predicate.
3
Work through the objection and refutation
Find the objection from al-Mawaqif: that the modalities discussed here are different from logical modalities, otherwise concomitants would be necessary in themselves. Then follow the author's two-part response: either meaning the concomitants themselves (denied) or the quiddities (accepted).
The refutation relies on the distinction just made.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموصوف بالذاتي إما واجب أو ممتنع
قال: والموصوف بالذاتي واجب الوجود لذاته، أو ممتنع الوجود لذاته، إن أخذ الوجود محمولاً، وواجب الوجود لشيء، وممتنع الوجود له نظراً إلى ذاته إن أخذ رابطة فلازم الماهية، كزوجية الأربعة واجب الوجود لها لذاتها، لا واجب الوجود لذاته.

يعني إذا أخذ الوجود محمولاً، فالموصوف بالوجوب الذاتي يكون واجب الوجود لذاته كالباري تعالى وبالامتناع الذاتي يكون ممتنع الوجود لذاته، كاجتماع النقيضين، وإذا أخذ رابطة بين الموضوع والمحمول فالموصوف بالوجوب الذاتي، يكون واجب الوجود لموضوعه نظراً إلى ذات الموضوع كالزوجية للأربعة وبالامتناع الذاتي يكون ممتنع الوجود له نظراً إليه، كالفردية للأربعة، فلازم الماهية كالزوجية مثلاً واجب الوجود لذاتها. أي واجب الثبوت للماهية نظراً إلى نفسها لا واجب الوجود لذاته، بمعنى اقتضائه الوجود بالذات ليلزم المحال وبهذا يسقط ما ذكر في المواقف من أن الوجوب، والإمكان، والامتناع، المبحوث عنها هنا غير الوجوب، والإمكان والامتناع التي هي جهات القضايا وموادها، وإلا لكانت لوازم الماهيات واجبة لذاتها، وذلك لأنه إن أراد كونها واجبة لذات اللوازم فالملازمة ممنوعة، أو لذات الماهيات فبطلان التالي ممنوع، فإن معناه أنها واجبة الثبوت للماهية نظراً إلى ذاتها من غير احتياج إلى أمر آخر، وكأنه يجعل بعض القضايا خلوا عن كون الوجود فيه محمولاً أو رابطة. كقولنا:
The thing described by the essential is either necessary or impossible. He said: That which is described by the essential is necessary of existence in itself, or impossible of existence in itself, if existence is taken as a predicate; and necessary of existence for something, and impossible of existence for it with respect to its essence, if it is taken as a copula. Then the necessary concomitant of the quiddity, like evenness for four, is necessary of existence for it by its essence, not necessary of existence in itself. That is: if existence is taken as a predicate, then what is described by essential necessity is necessary of existence in itself, like God most high, and by essential impossibility is impossible of existence in itself, like the conjunction of two contradictories. And if it is taken as a copula between subject and predicate, then what is described by essential necessity is necessary of existence for its subject with respect to the subject's essence, like evenness for four, and by essential impossibility is impossible of existence for it with respect to it, like oddness for four. So the necessary concomitant of the quiddity, like evenness for example, is necessary of existence for its quiddity, i.e., necessary of affirmation for the quiddity with respect to itself, not necessary of existence in itself, meaning requiring existence by essence — which would entail absurdity. And by this is refuted what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif, that the necessity, possibility, and impossibility discussed here are other than the necessity, possibility, and impossibility that are the modalities and matter of propositions; otherwise, the concomitants of quiddities would be necessary in themselves. And that is because if he intends them to be necessary in themselves with respect to the concomitants, then the implication is denied; or with respect to the quiddities, then the falsity of the consequent is denied, for its meaning is that they are necessary of affirmation for the quiddity with respect to itself without needing anything else. And it is as if he makes some propositions devoid of existence being either a predicate or a copula. Like our saying:
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between two ways of considering existence: as a predicate (mahmul) or as a copula (rabita) connecting subject and predicate. The author first states that when existence is taken as a predicate, the thing described by essential necessity (wajib dhati) is necessary in itself (like God), and the thing described by essential impossibility is impossible in itself (like the conjunction of contradictories). When existence is taken as a copula, essential necessity means that the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject due to the subject's own essence (e.g., evenness is necessarily true of four), and essential impossibility means the predicate is necessarily denied. Thus, the necessary concomitants (lawazim al-mahiyyat) are necessary for their quiddities, not necessary in themselves. The author then refutes an objection from al-Mawaqif which claimed that the modalities discussed here (essential necessity, etc.) are different from the modalities of propositions (wajib, mumkin, mumtani' as used in logic). The objection is that if they were the same, the necessary concomitants would be necessary in themselves, which is false. The author responds by analyzing the two possible interpretations: if by 'necessary in themselves' one means the concomitants themselves, that is false; if one means the quiddities, then it is true that the predicates are necessarily affirmed of them, and this does not make the concomitants necessary in themselves. Thus, the modalities discussed in kalam are indeed the same as the modalities of propositions, just considered from a different angle. The passage ends with an incomplete example ('like our saying:').

Key Terms
محمول
رابطة
لازم الماهية
واجب الوجود لذاته
واجب الوجود لشيء
جهات القضايا
مواد القضايا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) always means that something exists necessarily in itself, regardless of context.
Correction: As the passage shows, 'wajib al-wujud' can also mean necessary predication: a predicate is necessarily true of a subject due to the subject's essence, without the predicate having independent existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when existence is taken as a copula, the 'wajib al-wujud li dhatihi' (necessary of existence in itself) refers to what?
A) The subject itself, e.g., God
B) The predicate affirmed of the subject due to the subject's essence
C) The proposition as a whole
D) The concomitant in itself

Key Takeaway
The same modal terms (wajib, mumtani') can refer either to the existence of a subject in itself (predicate usage) or to the necessary truth of a proposition about a subject (copula usage).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you treat many things as 'necessary'—your routine, your relationships, your career. But according to this lesson, only God is 'necessary of existence in itself'. How does that truth expose the false gods you cling to, and what would it cost you to let them go?
?
The passage shows that 'necessary of existence for something' is not the same as 'necessary in itself'. When you say 'I must succeed', are you treating success as if it were necessary in itself? What would change if you saw it as only necessary for something transient?
?
This lesson refines how we think about modalities—does your heart actually live as though God is necessary in Himself, or do you treat Him as merely necessary for your preferred outcomes? What specific evidence shows your real belief?

The Twofold Manner of Taking Existence: Predicate vs Copula
Al-Taftazani distinguishes essential necessity and impossibility based on two ways of taking existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the role of existence in a proposition—as a predicate or as a copula—determines the meaning of essential necessity and impossibility, and why concomitants of quiddities are not necessary in themselves like God.

Core Idea
Existence's logical role changes whether necessity is absolute or relative to a subject.

In this passage, al-Taftazani refines our understanding of the rational judgments by attending to a subtle but crucial distinction: the way existence is taken in a proposition. If we say 'something is necessary in itself' versus 'something is necessary for a subject,' we are not using 'existence' in the same logical role. This clarification resolves a potential confusion about the necessity of concomitants and defends the coherence of the kalam framework against objections from al-Mawaqif.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once to let its rhythm sink in, then read the literal translation. Note the parallel structure: 'if existence is taken as a predicate... if taken as a copula...'
The passage is dense; do not worry about understanding every detail immediately.
2
Focus on the two scenarios
Identify the two cases: existence as predicate (mahmul) and existence as copula (rabita). Notice how the same attribute (e.g., evenness) can be necessary in one sense but not in another.
The key is that 'necessary in itself' applies only when existence is a predicate attached to the essence.
3
Understand the refutation of al-Mawaqif
Al-Taftazani uses this distinction to answer an objection. What is the objection and how does his distinction solve it?
If concomitants were necessary in themselves, they would be like God—but they are only necessary for their quiddity.
4
Appreciate the final clarification on predication
The passage ends by noting that expressions like 'exists for him' and 'is predicated' are equivalent in meaning. This unifies the terminology.
This connects to the logical analysis of propositions like 'Man is a writer.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموصوف بالذاتي إما واجب أو ممتنع قال ؛ والموصوف بالذاتي واجب الوجود لذاته، أو ممتنع الوجود لذاته، إن أخذ الوجود محمولاً ، وواجب الوجود لشيء، وممتنع الوجود له نظراً إلى ذاته إن أخذ رابطة فلازم الماهية ، كزوجية الأربعة واجب الوجود لها (۱) لذاتها، لا واجب الوجود لذاته . يعني إذا أخذ الوجود محمولاً ، فالموصوف بالوجوب (٢) الذاتي يكون واجب الوجود لذاته كالباري تعالى وبالامتناع الذاتي يكون ممتنع الوجود لذاته، كاجتماع النقيضين، وإذا أخذ رابطة بين الموضوع والمحمول فالموصوف (۳) بالوجوب الذاتي، يكون واجب الوجود لموضـوعـه نظراً إلى ذات الموضوع كالزوجية للأربعة وبالامتناع الذاتي يكون ممتنع الوجود لـه نظراً إليه، كالفردية للأربعة، فلازم الماهية كالزوجية مثلاً واجب الوجود لذاتها. أي واجب الثبوت للماهية نظراً إلى نفسها لا واجب الوجود لذاته، بمعنى اقتضائه الوجود بالذات ليلزم المحال وبهذا يسقط ما ذكر في المواقف من أن الوجوب، والإمكان ، والامتناع ، المبحوث عنهـا هنـا غـيـر الـوجـوب، والإمكان والامتناع التي هي جهات القضايا وموادها، وإلا لكانت لوازم الماهيات واجبة لذاتها، و وذلك لأنه إن أراد كونها واجبة لذات اللوازم فالملازمة ممنوعة ، أو لذات الماهيات فبطلان التالي ممنوع، فإن معناه أنها واجبة الثبوت للماهية نظراً إلى ذاتها من غير احتياج إلى أمر آخر، وكأنه يجعل بعض القضايا خلوا عن كون الوجود فيه محمولاً أو رابطة . كقولنا : الإنسان كاتب، ويمتنع (١) أن يكون معناه أنه يوجد كاتباً أو توجد له الكتابة : بل معناه أن ما صدق عليه هذا يصدق عليه ذاك أو يحمل . والمحققون على أنه لا فرق بين قولنا : يوجد له ذاك (۲) ، ويثبت، ويصدق عليه، ويحمل ونحو ذلك إلا بحسب العبارة (۳) ، وما ذكرنا هو الموافق لكلام المحقق في التجريد.
The attribute qualified by the essential is either necessary or impossible. He said: And the attribute qualified by the essential is necessary of existence in itself, or impossible of existence in itself, if existence is taken as a predicate, and necessary of existence for something, and impossible of existence for it in view of its essence if taken as a copula. So the concomitant of the quiddity, like the evenness of four, is necessary of existence for it (1) in itself, not necessary of existence in itself. That is, if existence is taken as a predicate, then the attribute qualified by essential necessity is necessary of existence in itself like the Exalted Creator, and by essential impossibility is impossible of existence in itself like the conjunction of opposites. And if taken as a copula between subject and predicate, then the attribute qualified by essential necessity is necessary of existence for its subject in view of the subject's essence, like evenness for four, and by essential impossibility is impossible of existence for it in view of it, like oddness for four. So the concomitant of the quiddity, like evenness for example, is necessary of existence for itself, i.e., necessary of affirmation for the quiddity in view of itself, not necessary of existence in itself, meaning its requiring existence in essence, so that an impossibility would follow. And by this is refuted what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif: that necessity, possibility, and impossibility discussed here are other than necessity, possibility, and impossibility which are the modalities and matters of propositions; otherwise the concomitants of quiddities would be necessary in themselves. And that is because if He intended them to be necessary in themselves as concomitants, the implication is denied; or in themselves as quiddities, then the falsity of the consequent is denied, for its meaning is that they are necessarily affirmed for the quiddity in view of itself without need for another matter. And it is as if some propositions are devoid of existence as a predicate or copula, like our statement: 'Man is a writer.' And it is impossible that its meaning be 'he exists as a writer' or 'writing exists for him'; rather its meaning is that what this applies to, that applies to it, or it is predicated. And the verifying scholars hold that there is no difference between our statements: 'exists for him', 'is established', 'applies to', 'is predicated' and the like except in terms of expression. And what we have mentioned is in agreement with the statement of the verifying scholar in al-Tajrid.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the distinction between essential necessity and impossibility when existence is taken in two different logical roles: as a predicate or as a copula. The author begins by stating that the attribute qualified by the essential (al-mawsuf bi al-dhati) is either necessary or impossible. Then he explains: if existence is taken as a predicate (mahmul), then the attribute qualified by essential necessity is necessary of existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) like God, and by essential impossibility is impossible of existence in itself like the conjunction of opposites. But if existence is taken as a copula (rabita) linking subject and predicate, then the attribute qualified by essential necessity becomes necessary of existence for its subject (wajib al-wujud li-mawdu'ihi) in view of the subject's essence, as evenness is for four, and essential impossibility becomes impossible of existence for it, as oddness is for four. Thus the concomitant of a quiddity (lazim al-mahiyya), like evenness for four, is necessary of existence for itself, meaning necessary of affirmation for the quiddity in view of itself, not necessary of existence in itself as an independent entity. This refutes a view from al-Mawaqif that the modalities discussed here are different from the modalities of propositions; otherwise concomitants would be necessary in themselves. The author then argues that the apparent issue arises only if one interprets 'necessary in themselves' as necessary in themselves as concomitants or as quiddities, both of which are denied or permissible. He further explains that some propositions are devoid of existence as a predicate or copula, like 'Man is a writer,' where the meaning is simply that the subject and predicate apply to each other. The verifying scholars hold that expressions like 'exists for him', 'is established', 'applies to', 'is predicated' are equivalent in meaning, differing only in phrasing. The passage ends by affirming that this explanation agrees with the statement of the verifying scholar in al-Tajrid.

الوجوب الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي
المحمول
الرابطة
لازم الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the necessity of concomitants (e.g., evenness for four) is the same as the necessity of God's existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that concomitants are necessary of existence for themselves only in the sense of being necessarily affirmed of the quiddity, not as independently necessary existents. God's necessity is absolute and in Himself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when existence is taken as a copula, what does 'necessary existence' mean for a subject?
A) Necessary existence in itself
B) Necessity of a predicate for a subject relative to the subject's essence
C) Necessary existence for something else
D) Necessary existence as a concomitant of another

Key Takeaway
Essential necessity and impossibility have two distinct meanings depending on whether existence functions as a predicate (absolute) or as a copula (relative to a subject), and this distinction preserves the unique status of God's necessity.

Proof that Possibility is Essential, not Accidental
Al-Taftazani proves by reductio that possibility must be essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani proves that possibility must be an essential attribute of a thing, not an accidental one, using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
Possibility must be essential, else reality would contradict itself.

Al-Taftazani now tackles a crucial distinction: is possibility intrinsic to a thing's nature, or does it come from outside? This short but powerful proof shows that if possibility were accidental, it would require an absurd contradiction. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The passage begins with the thesis, then supposes the opposite. Find the assumption (accidental possibility) and trace how it leads to a contradiction.
The contradiction is called 'conversion' (inqilab).
3
Understand the term 'inqilab'
Conversion here means that an essential property would be removed, which is impossible. This is the heart of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والإمكان ذاتي لا غير . إذ لو كان غيرياً لكان الشيء في نفسه واجباً أو ممتنعاً، أي ضروري الوجود أو العدم بالذات ثم يصير لا ضروري الوجود والعدم بالغير، فيرتفع ما بالذات، وهو محال بالضرورة، وهذا معنى الانقلاب.
He said: And possibility is essential, not accidental. For if it were accidental, then the thing in itself would be necessary or impossible, i.e., necessary in existence or non-existence in essence, then it would become not necessary in existence or non-existence accidentally, so what is in essence would be removed, and that is impossible necessarily. And this is the meaning of conversion.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage provides a proof by contradiction that possibility (imkan) must be essential (dhati) and cannot be accidental (ghairi). The author begins by stating that possibility is essential, not accidental. To prove this, he considers the contrary: if possibility were accidental, then a thing would have an intrinsic nature that is either necessary or impossible in itself (i.e., essential necessity or impossibility), but then by an accidental attribute it would become not necessary or not impossible. This would remove what is essential, which is necessarily impossible. This impossibility is called 'conversion' (inqilab). Therefore, possibility cannot be accidental; it must be essential to the thing's nature.

Key Terms
الإمكان الذاتي
الإمكان الغيري
الانقلاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility can be an accidental property of a thing.
Correction: The passage shows that if possibility were accidental, it would require removing an essential attribute, which is logically impossible. Therefore, possibility must be essential.

Checkpoint
What would happen if possibility (imkan) were accidental (ghairi)?
A) The thing would become necessary
B) The essential property would be removed, which is impossible
C) It would be the same as impossibility
D) It would require a cause

Key Takeaway
Possibility (imkan) is an essential attribute; if accidental, it would cause an impossible contradiction (inqilab).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is merely possible (contingent) in itself, not necessary, then do you live as if your life could be taken away at any moment? How would you change your priorities today if you fully internalized that you have no claim to existence on your own?
?
The proof shows that an essential attribute cannot be removed by an accidental one. What is the essential attribute of your relationship with Allah—is it a central, unshakeable reality, or do you treat it as something accidental, subject to your moods and circumstances? What concrete step can you take to make your faith more essential to your daily life?
?
Consider that if possibility were accidental, a thing could change its very nature. Does your life reflect a stable identity as a servant of Allah, or do you allow temporary desires to 'convert' you away from that identity? What will you commit to reminding yourself of your true essential nature?
